201 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Weir


No. 1679 Pittsburgh 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 210; 738 A.2d 467; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2768



August 18, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence August 17, 1998. In the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County. Criminal No. CC98-02574. Before BIGLEY, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), denying his motion to suppress blood alcohol content results and convicting him of driving under the influence under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 3731(a)(1) and (4).


OVERVIEW: Officer stopped appellant after his car swerved across the road. Appellant failed various field sobriety tests and agreed to a blood alcohol test. His blood alcohol content (BAC) was above the legal limit. The trial court denied appellant's motion to suppress the BAC results. Appellant was convicted of driving under the influence (DUI) under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 3731(a)(1) and (4). Appellant sought review, claiming the prosecution should have presented relation-back testimony, this was the first time officer had effectuated a DUI arrest by himself, and the trial court improperly limited defense counsel's cross-examination of officer regarding the standing leg test. The court affirmed because the prosecution was not required to present expert relation-back testimony, officer's inexperience did not warrant reversal, and the trial court's refusal to permit officer from demonstrating the standing leg test did not require a new trial since evidence of appellant's guilt was overwhelming.


OUTCOME: Denial of appellant's motion to suppress blood alcohol content results and his conviction of driving under the influence were affirmed because prosecution did not have to present expert relation-back testimony, and the trial court's refusal to permit officer from demonstrating the standing leg test did not require a new trial.

 

202 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Dehart


No. 471 Harrisburg 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 104; 730 A.2d 991; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 839


January 25, 1999, Submitted 
May 5, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 14, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 3138.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the PCRA ORDER March 3, 1998, Docketed March 9, 1998, in the Court of Common Pleas of HUNTINGDON County, CRIMINAL, No. 83 C A 144. Before QUIGLEY, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a convicted felon, sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County (Pennsylvania), dismissing his PCRA petition for untimeliness. On appeal, appellant contended that the trial judge failed to afford him an opportunity to file objections in violation of an earlier directive of this court.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder, robbery, burglary, and escape. A jury imposed the death penalty. Before the court was appellant's second PCRA petition, which challenged the conviction and sentence. The first PCRA petition resulted in appellant being sentenced to life imprisonment. The petition was dismissed without the requisite 10-day notice of intent to dismiss given to appellant. Appellant sought review and this court remanded. The trial court thereafter issued a notice of intent to dismiss and allowed appellant 45 days to file objections. Following a lapse of the 45 days, the petition was dismissed. Appellant sought review in this court, contending that the trial judge failed to afford him an opportunity to file objections in violation of this court's earlier directive. The court held that appellant's PCRA petition was untimely pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9545 because it was not filed within one year of the date his judgment of conviction became final. Thus, the trial court lacked jurisdiction to consider the petition's merits. The court further ruled that none of the exceptions that would make the timeliness rule inapplicable applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's PCRA petition. The court held that because the petition was not filed within one year of the date his judgment of conviction became final, the trial court lacked jurisdiction to consider the petition. The court also ruled that none of the exceptions to the timeliness rule applied.

 

203 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Smith


No. 2 Philadelphia 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 96; 732 A.2d 1226; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 819



April 26, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted April 10, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Pa. LEXIS 721.
Reargument denied by, 06/30/1999
Appeal granted by Commonwealth v. Smith, 565 Pa. 643, 771 A.2d 1283, 2001 Pa. LEXIS 721 (2001)
Affirmed by Commonwealth v. Smith, 2003 Pa. LEXIS 2153 (Pa., Nov. 19, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence December 5, 1997 In the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County Criminal No. 6523-96. Before SUBERS, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of the Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), convicting defendant of possession of a controlled substance, intent to deliver, and possession of marijuana, resulting from a police drug interdiction investigation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's drug conviction stemmed from a drug interdiction investigation performed at a bus terminal. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence seized by the police, as well as the statements defendant made to the officers. The appellate court held that defendant's initial contact with the police was a mere encounter. The court found that defendant voluntarily abandoned the bag containing the cocaine because the contact involved no police coercion. Additionally, defendant voluntarily revealed her intentions to one of the officers, and after defendant told him that she was delivering drugs, she was properly Mirandized. Finally, the statements made by defendant prior to being given her Miranda warnings were correctly allowed as evidence because defendant was not in custody when the statements were made.


OUTCOME: Defendant's drug conviction was affirmed because the trial court correctly denied her motion to suppress the evidence seized by the police, as well as the statements she made to the officers during a drug interdiction investigation that was conducted at a bus terminal.

Commonwealth v. DuPont


No. 4505 Philadelphia, 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 88; 730 A.2d 970; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 813


March 9, 1999, Argued  
April 20, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied June 12, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 4038. Motion for Admission pro hac vice Granted and Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 13, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Pa. LEXIS 129.
Appeal denied by, Petition granted by Commonwealth v. DuPont, 561 Pa. 669, 749 A.2d 466, 2000 Pa. LEXIS 129 (2000)
Writ of certiorari denied DuPont v. Pennsylvania, 530 U.S. 1231, 120 S. Ct. 2663, 147 L. Ed. 2d 276, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 4038 (2000)
Post-conviction relief denied at Commonwealth v. duPont, 2004 PA Super 364, 860 A.2d 525, 2004 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3292 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of May 13, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County, Criminal Division, at No. 26-96. Before JENKINS, J.
Commonwealth v. duPont, 545 Pa. 564, 681 A.2d 1328, 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1667 (1996)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) after a jury found him guilty of murder and assault but mentally ill, arguing that a prosecution expert's testimony violated the psychiatrist-patient privilege and the attorney-client privilege. Appellant also challenged the validity of the insanity defense statutes, 18 Pa. Con. Stat. §§ 314, 315.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who resided on an estate in which he housed wrestlers who were members of his team, challenged a judgment finding him mentally ill, but guilty of murder and simple assault. Witnesses testified that appellant's behavior changed after his mother died and that he exhibited paranoid fear that he was being spied on and that his life was in danger. Appellant subsequently shot and killed a wrestling coach to whom he had exhibited animosity. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the state's insanity test, arguing that the burden was on the prosecution to prove that he was not insane and knew right from wrong. Appellant also argued that the trial court violated both the psychiatrist-patient and attorney-client privileges by allowing the testimony of an expert with a previous connection to appellant. Holding that the allocation of the burden of proof in an insanity defense had been clearly defined and that challenges to its constitutionality had previously been rejected, the court affirmed the judgment because application of the privileges required confidential communications or relationships that were protectable.


OUTCOME: Concluding there was no merit to appellant's claims that the state's statutory scheme for determining mental illness was unconstitutional, the court affirmed the judgment because there was no confidential relationship between appellant and the challenged expert psychiatrist, and because the record did not support the conclusion that the attorney-client privilege was violated in any way.

Commonwealth v. Roman


No. 276 Philadelphia 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 79; 730 A.2d 486; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 361


February 9, 1999, Argued  
April 14, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 2, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 3595.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order December 12, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County Criminal No. 9412-0331. Before GLAZER, J.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) order that granted appellee criminal's petition under the Post Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 9541-9546, because five days after he was sentenced, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a)(5), one of the statutes that appellee was convicted of violating, was declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state challenged a trial court decision that granted appellee criminal's petition under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 9541-9546, because one of the statutes that appellee was convicted of violating and sentenced under, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a)(5), was declared unconstitutional five days after appellee was sentenced. The appellate court reversed the decision of the trial court. Appellee's PCRA petition was untimely under § 9545(b) because it was not filed within one year from the date that appellee's judgment of sentence became final. No exception to excuse the untimely filing existed because appellee had ample time to file either a direct appeal or a timely PCRA petition. Even if a PCRA claim was timely filed, the claim was waived because the issue could have been previously waived.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed a trial court decision that granted appellee criminal's Post Conviction Relief Act petition challenging conviction after part of the statute appellee was convicted under was declared unconstitutional five days after appellee was sentenced. Appellee was not entitled to his requested relief because his petition was untimely because it was not filed within a year of final sentencing and the issue had been waived.

Middleton/DPW v. Robinson


No. 944 Harrisburg 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 70; 728 A.2d 368; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 314


December 16, 1998, Argued  
April 6, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered December 11, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Civil Division at No. 1007 SUP ACTION 1986 DRO NUMBER 35773. Before BRILLHART, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Contending that appellee Department of Public Welfare lacked standing to sue for support and an assignment of a right of a child to support was unconstitutional, appellant noncustodial parent challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which retroactively awarded child support from the time appellee instituted paternity suit against appellant. Appellant also raised procedural due process challenges.


OVERVIEW: Appellant noncustodial parent challenged an order that retroactively awarded child support from the time appellee welfare department instituted paternity suit, although appellant acknowledged paternity after nearly ten years of litigation. Appellant argued appellee lacked standing because child support rights could not be assigned to a third party, including a governmental agency. Appellant also argued portions of his support payment could be used for the support of siblings not related to him. Appellant contended procedural due process was violated in the manner the child support matter was prosecuted in violation of the separation of powers and other constitutional safeguards. Because substantial defects in appellant's brief precluded effective appellate review of his due process violation claims, the appellate court deemed those claims waived. The court also dismissed appellant's other claims because appellee's standing to enforce an unwed recipient's right to receive child support payments derived from state statute, and the requirement that support income be used for the benefit of the child did not preclude its use for common expenses.


OUTCOME: Holding substantial defects in appellant's brief precluded effective appellate review of his procedural due process claims, the court dismissed appellant's other challenges because appellee possessed statutory authority to enforce appellant's support obligation, and the lower court properly refused to deviate from the support guidelines based upon the mother's status as a welfare recipient.

Commonwealth v. Wilmington


No. 2790 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 66; 729 A.2d 1160; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 824



March 31, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 27, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Pa. LEXIS 614.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence June 25, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Criminal, No. 6 Criminal 1997. Before MILLER, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence vacated. Case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an appeal from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania), the court granted the prosecution's petition for reargument to determine whether Pa. Const. art. I, § 8, prohibited the random stopping of a Greyhound bus to permit police officers to conduct a drug interdiction investigation, in the absence of reasonable suspicion or probable cause to believe that an individual on the bus was transporting narcotics.


OVERVIEW: Appellant convicted on drug charges contended on reargument that the state constitution prohibited the random stopping of a Greyhound bus at a toll plaza on a rural interstate to permit police to conduct a drug interdiction investigation in the absence of reasonable suspicion or probable cause to believe that an individual on the bus was transporting narcotics. With the permission of the bus driver, police boarded the bus and searched two bags. Appellant acknowledged the first was his, and it contained a brand-new pair of sneakers; the second bag, to which no one acknowledged ownership, contained a shoe box full of drugs that had held the identical size as appellant's pair of sneakers. Appellant contended the evidence should have been suppressed because the search and seizure was illegal. The prosecution contended the seriousness of the criminal activity under investigation justified the intrusion. The court disagreed and reversed the conviction, holding that the state constitutional requirement of a reasonable articulable suspicion to support an investigative seizure protected appellant's right to be free of arbitrary police intrusions on his privacy and free movement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction after determining appellant was unconstitutionally seized, holding that clear precedent defined the appropriate standards to be used when considering whether an individual had been seized, and the long-standing definition of what constituted a seizure could not be ignored, particularly when viewed in tandem with recognition of the privacy rights embodied in Pa. Const. art. I, § 8.

Flora v. Moses


No. 103 Harrisburg, 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 60; 727 A.2d 596; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 198


October 7, 1998, Argued  
March 19, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 21, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2137.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of January 13, 1998, In the Court of Common Pleas, Luzerne County, Civil Division, at No. 1119-C of 1996. Before Augello, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), in which the court dismissed appellant's complaint finding that the Health Care Services Malpractice Act (HCSMA), 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 606, barred recovery because there was no written contract.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brought suit against appellee doctor alleging appellee doctor breached a contract guaranteeing a cure. The trial court dismissed appellant's complaint finding that the Health Care Services Malpractice Act (HCSMA), 40 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 606, barred recovery, because there was no written contract. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. According to the superior court, section 606 required a writing in order to hold a health care provider liable as a warrantor or guarantor of a cure. The superior court held that appellant did not have a written contract and could not pursue its action. The superior court rejected the argument that section 606 was unconstitutional. The superior court held that section 606 was a limited and particularized statute of frauds addressing a certain type of contractual scenario and there was nothing unconstitutional about the provision. The superior court also rejected the argument that appellee doctor waived its affirmative defense by failing to allege it. The superior court held that was proffered by a party at a later stage of the proceedings and was considered by the court: the court's consideration was treated as approving an amendment.


OUTCOME: The superior court affirmed the judgment dismissing appellant's complaint because appellant did not have a written contract, and without a written contract, appellee doctor could not be held liable as a guarantor or warrantor of a cure.

Commonwealth v. Ketterer


No. 1283 Philadelphia 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 30; 725 A.2d 801; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 123


December 16, 1998, Argued  
February 17, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence November 20, 1997 In the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County Criminal No. 5850-96. Before KOUDELIS, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part, vacated in part and remanded. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which was entered following defendant's jury conviction for aggravated assault by vehicle while driving under the influence, driving under the influence, and recklessly endangering another person. Defendant claimed that there was insufficient evidence, as well as other alleged errors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant struck another vehicle while driving under the influence of alcohol and speeding during a severe rainstorm. The other driver and his passenger were severely injured, and defendant was subsequently convicted of aggravated assault by vehicle while driving under the influence, driving under the influence, and recklessly endangering another person. Defendant contended that there was insufficient evidence to convict him of aggravated assault because his bad judgment in inclement weather should be severable from evidence regarding his intoxication. The appellate court affirmed his conviction for driving under the influence and aggravated assault holding that the jury properly concluded that his driving under the influence started an unbroken chain of causation and directly precipitated the collision. Defendant's conviction for reckless endangerment was reversed, and the court held that the trial court committed reversible error by failing to instruct the jury on the issue of negligence and the level of negligence necessary for the behavior to constitute reckless endangerment. The action was remanded for a new trial on reckless endangerment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for driving under the influence and aggravated assault by vehicle while driving under the influence were affirmed. However, defendant's conviction for recklessly endangering another person was reversed where the appellate court found that the trial court failed to instruct the jury on the difference between reckless behavior and ordinary negligence.

Commonwealth v. Martin


No. 860 Pittsburgh 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 29; 727 A.2d 1136; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 121


December 8, 1998, Submitted  
February 16, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 5, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2996. Application for Reconsideration/Reargument Denied April 23, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Entered March 12, 1997 in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. CC 9608325. Before NOVAK, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed; allowance of appeal from discretionary aspects of sentence denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) convicting him of third-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his third-degree murder conviction arguing that the trial court erred in refusing to charge the jury with his requested jury instruction, erred in allowing the coroner to conduct an inquest, and abused its discretion in sentencing him above the guideline range without giving sufficient reasons. The appellate court appellant's conviction. The trial court accurately stated the law of justification to the jury as to the state's burden of proving appellant's reasonable belief that he was in imminent danger of death or great bodily harm and had to kill in order to save himself. It was not a constitutional violation to allow the coroner to conduct an inquest in lieu of a preliminary hearing. Appellant was given a fair, complete, and impartial trial on the merits before judgment was entered against him. Appellant's challenges to his sentence were to the discretionary aspects of the sentence and not the legality of the sentence. Appellant failed to show that there was a substantial question that the sentence was inappropriate. The trial court did not abuse its discretion by giving appellant the maximum sentence without providing sufficient reasons.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The appellate court held that the trial court's charge to the jury clearly, accurately, and adequately reflected the law of justification and was sufficient to guide the jury through its deliberations and further held that the trial court did not err in allowing the coroner to conduct an inquest in lieu of a preliminary hearing.

 

211 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Aziz


No. 3666 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 12; 724 A.2d 371; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 15



January 22, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Dated August 11, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal No. 96-10-0137.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) denying defendant's decertification request under the Juvenile Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 6301-6365.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Juvenile Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 6301-6365, defendant's case went directly to criminal court, but defendant had the option of requesting decertification of his case to the juvenile system because he was seventeen. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the act. The court upheld the denial of decertification and constitutionality of the act. The act's provisions were rationally related to its purpose of protecting the community, imposing accountability on young offenders and developing responsible members of society. The act's presumption of adult treatment for certain offenses did not create an irrebuttable presumption because a defendant was provided a hearing to rebut the adult treatment. Defendant was not denied due process by having the burden placed on him for decertification because the decision to transfer had no bearing on either the procedural or substantive aspects of the case. Juveniles were not a protected class and thus were not deprived of fundamental rights under the act. In light of defendant's violent history, the court found denial of decertification proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision to deny decertification and rejected defendant's constitutional challenges to the Juvenile Act. The Juvenile Act's provisions were rationally related to its goal of imposing accountability on young offenders. Decertification was properly denied based on defendant's violent history and previous futile encounters with the juvenile system.

Commonwealth v. Baker


No. 586 Harrisburg 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


722 A.2d 718; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4648


September 9, 1998, Argued  
December 23, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered May 29, 1997 in the Court of Common Pleas of York County Criminal, No. 170 MA 1997. Before BLACKWELL, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to six months' incarceration for his conviction of indirect criminal contempt.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's former girlfriend filed a petition for protection from abuse. The trial court issued an ex parte temporary order under the provisions of the Protection From Abuse Act (PFA), 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6101-6118. The order prohibited appellant from threatening or placing plaintiff in fear of abuse. Appellant was incarcerated at the time police officers served him with the order. After it was read and explained to him, appellant stated "I'm going to kill this bitch." The statement was not reported to plaintiff, but the officers did file an indirect criminal contempt charge against appellant. After a hearing, appellant was found guilty and sentenced to six months' incarceration. The court vacated the judgment of sentence and relinquished jurisdiction because appellant did not have sufficient notice that a statement lacking any measure of immediacy, made outside the presence of the person to whom it was directed, and which did not cause the protected person to feel threatened constituted a violation of the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment that convicted appellant of indirect criminal contempt and sentenced him to six months' incarceration because appellant did not have notice that a threat made outside the presence of the victim and which did not put her in fear of abuse, constituted a violation of the trial court's temporary protection order.

 

213 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sadvari


No. 705 Philadelphia 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


723 A.2d 1044; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3903


September 24, 1998, Argued  
December 14, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted July 22, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2153.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence January 26, 1998, In the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County Criminal Division, No. 6239-96. Before KEELER, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the denial of his motion to suppress evidence by the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of driving under the influence and driving with a blood alcohol count of .10 percent or greater. Defendant contended that the arresting officer did not follow Del. Code Ann. tit.11, § 1933 in extraditing him, and evidence obtained from the arrest should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: A Pennsylvania state trooper observed defendant driving in an unsafe manner. By the time he "pulled over" defendant, they had crossed the state line into Delaware. Defendant showed signs of intoxication and failed two field sobriety tests. Defendant was arrested for driving while intoxicated and taken to a hospital for blood alcohol tests, which confirmed that his blood alcohol count was above the legal limit. Defendant was then taken before a district justice in Pennsylvania, who ruled that sufficient probable cause existed to justify the arrest. Defendant made a pretrial motion to suppress evidence obtained in what he alleged was an illegal arrest, but that motion was denied. On appeal defendant contended that his arrest was illegal because the state trooper had not followed the extradition procedure required by Del. Code Ann. tit. 11, § 1933. The court agreed that the state trooper violated the extradition statute, but held that this violation did not touch upon defendant's fundamental constitutional rights. The court found that the exclusionary provisions of Pa. R. Crim. P. 323 did not apply in this case, and that the trial court correctly denied defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of driving under the influence and driving with a blood alcohol count of .10 percent or greater, holding that the trial court committed no error in denying his pre-trial motion to suppress evidence. The court held that the arresting officer's violation of an extradition rule did not compromise defendant's constitutional rights and thus did not warrant automatic application of the exclusionary rule.

 

214 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Van Rice


No. 289 Harrisburg 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


721 A.2d 1119; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3900


August 31, 1998, Submitted  
December 10, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 25, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 1510.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE January 30, 1998 In the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal No. 921 C A 1997. Before KENNEDY, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of possession with the intent to deliver cocaine.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to being convicted of possession with the intent to deliver cocaine, defendant argued that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence. Defendant contended that the parole officer that found the cocaine jacket was not authorized to conduct a warrantless search of defendant. The court acknowledged that a parole officer was not authorized to conduct a warrantless search in the absence of the consent of the parolee or a statutory or regulatory framework authorizing such a search. However, the court held that 61 P.S. § 331.27a allowed a parole officer to search a parolee that was lawfully taken into custody. As defendant had been properly taken into custody prior to the search, the court held that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession with intent to deliver cocaine, as a parole officer had the legal authority to search appellant after taking him into custody. Thus, the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence.

 

215 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Rohrer


No. 563 Harrisburg 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


719 A.2d 1078; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3286



November 9, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence November 10, 1997. In the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County Criminal No. 1061-1997. Before BUCHER, J.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Criminal Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) which modified his 6-23 month sentence for driving under the influence, receiving stolen property, fleeing and eluding the police, and various summary offenses, by imposing restitution.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to 6-23 months imprisonment in addition to a fine and other costs, after he entered an open plea to driving under the influence, receiving stolen property, fleeing and eluding the police, and various summary offenses. Appellant challenged the order, which granted the Commonwealth's motion to amend sentence, filed three and a half months after imposition of sentence, and modified the sentence to include restitution, on the basis that the motion was untimely and the court was without jurisdiction to amend his sentence. The court vacated the order. The failure to file a motion to reconsider sentence within 10 days after the imposition of sentence waived any complaint concerning the sentence that did not involve the lawfulness of the sentence itself under Pa. R. Crim. P. 1410(a). Though the trial court could sua sponte modify or rescind a prior order pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §5505, the imposition of restitution for property damage was not mandatory under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1106(a), (c)(1)(i). As such, appellant's sentence was not illegal and the commonwealth was required to file a timely post-sentence motion to challenge the sentence.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order that modified appellant's sentence to include restitution because the sentencing court was without jurisdiction to entertain the commonwealth's untimely motion to amend the sentence.

 

216 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Eddings


No. 1144 Harrisburg 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


721 A.2d 1095; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3284


September 1, 1998, Argued  
November 6, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted February 16, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Pa. LEXIS 346.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, June 2, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal, No. 1410 CD 1996. Before LEWIS, J.



DISPOSITION: Sentence vacated. Remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), that imposed a mandatory sentence for third degree murder pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714(a)(2) because the offense was his third conviction for a crime of violence.


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, appellant was convicted of third degree murder and given a mandatory sentence pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714(a)(2) because the offense was his third conviction for a crime of violence. On appeal, the court vacated the judgment of sentence, holding that the trial court erred in concluding that appellant's prior convictions were exempt from the statutory requirement that only those prior convictions occurring within seven years of the current offense be included for mandatory sentencing. The court determined that under well-settled principles of statutory construction, the seven-year requirement applied to sentences imposed under both 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714(a)1 and (2). Thus, the court remanded the cause to the trial court for a determination of whether appellant had been convicted of a prior crime of violence with seven years of the current offense. The court also concluded that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in concluding that the prosecutor proffered legitimate race neutral reasons for striking jurors.


OUTCOME: The court vacated a mandatory sentence for third degree murder when the offense was his third conviction for a crime of violence because the trial court erred in refusing to apply the seven-year limitation to prior violent offenses for mandatory sentencing. The cause was remanded to determine if appellant had been convicted of prior violent crimes within seven years of the current offense.

Commonwealth v. Kellam


No. 783 Pittsburgh 1998


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


719 A.2d 792; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2948


September 14, 1998, Submitted  
October 23, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 14, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2015.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County, Criminal Division, No. 1076 OF 1996. Before WAGNER, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County (Pennsylvania) for third degree murder of his girlfriend's infant. Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient and that the definition used for caregiver was unconstitutionally vague. Defendant also alleged prosecutorial misconduct, that jurors were stricken based on race, and that after-discovered evidence required a new trial.


OVERVIEW: The infant of defendant's girlfriend died from dehydration, malnutrition and hypothermia after being left in defendant's care. On appeal from his conviction for third degree murder, the court held that a person placed in control and supervision of a child has a duty to protect the child. Evidence that defendant was the only adult who could care for the infant and actively deceived the mother about its condition supported the jury's verdict. Defendant's intent to commit the crime charged was evidenced by his disregard of the fact that the infant consumed no food or fluids for 24-48 hours. The instruction on the definition of caregiver was based on statutory language and not constitutionally vague. The trial court adequately addressed any alleged instance of prosecutorial misconduct. No potential juror was stricken based on race since both black and white jurors were stricken for sleeping during voir dire. After-discovered evidence in the form of alibi testimony was not credible because defendant knew about the proposed witnesses prior to trial and had previously filed a notice of alibi based on entirely different facts. Accordingly, the judgment of sentence was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence for third degree murder of his girlfriend's infant was affirmed. Evidence supported the jury's verdict and established intent to commit the crime charged. Statutory language was used to define caregiver, and the trial court adequately addressed any instances of prosecutorial misconduct. Both black and white jurors were stricken for sleeping during voir dire, and after-discovered alibi evidence was not credible.

 

219 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Koller


No. 540 Harrisburg 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2950



October 23, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appeal from the JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE December 19, 1996. In the Court of Common Pleas of Bradford County, Criminal No. 96 CR 000474.



DISPOSITION: Vacated; Remanded for resentencing; Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the sentence imposed against him by the Court of Common Pleas of Bradford County (Pennsylvania) as a sexually violent predator under the Registration of Sexual Offender's Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9791-9799.6 (the Act). Defendant argued that the Act was unconstitutional because it placed the burden of persuasion on a defendant to rebut the presumption that he was a sexually violent predator under the Act.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found to be a "sexually violent predator" under the Registration of Sexual Offender's Act (Act), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9791-9799.6 and received an 18-60 month term. Defendant's post-sentence motion argued the Act was unconstitutional. A modified 18 months to life sentence was then imposed, and defendant's second post-sentence motion was denied. On review, defendant argued that the Act was unconstitutional because § 9794(b) placed the burden of persuasion on a defendant to rebut the presumption that he was a "sexually violent predator." The court disagreed, but noted that it was bound by a previous en banc decision which determined that § 9794(b) of the Act was unconstitutional. However, the court vehemently disagreed with the previous majority decision because it ignored the presumption of a statute's constitutionality. The court noted that § 9794(b) was no different than placing the burden of mitigation in sentencing on a defendant and reasoned that § 9794(b) did not violate due process of law. Nonetheless, in accordance with established precedent, the case was remanded for resentencing absent application of the provisions of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the sentence imposed upon defendant by the trial court as a "sexually violent predator." The matter remanded for resentencing absent application of the provisions of a sexual offender's statute. Although the court disagreed, it was bound by a previous en banc decision that held that the statute under which defendant was sentenced was unconstitutional.

Commonwealth v. Parker


No. 4279 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


718 A.2d 1266; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2859

DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which entered judgment of sentence following a jury trial in which defendant was convicted of a third robbery under a recidivist statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714. Defendant was sentenced to 25 to 50 years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: In this direct appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of a recidivist statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714. In bringing this challenge, defendant argued that his sentence amounted to cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Pa. Const. art. I, § 13. Defendant argued that his sentence was unconstitutionally disproportionate to the crime. On appeal, the court rejected defendant's arguments. The court rejected defendant's argument that since no actual violence occurred, the crime should not be considered violent. In light of defendant's third conviction for a violent crime, the court held that defendant's mandatory minimum sentence of 25 to 50 years was not so grossly disproportionate that it required further inquiry or analysis. The court held that the sentence did not violate the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment in U.S. Const. amend. VIII or Pa. Const. art. I, § 13. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence. The court held that in light of defendant's third conviction for a violent crime, defendant's mandatory minimum sentence of 25 to 50 years was not so grossly disproportionate that it required further inquiry or analysis. The court held that the sentence did not violate the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.

Commonwealth v. Halye


No. 2183 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


719 A.2d 763; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2820



October 6, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certoiorari Denied March 6, 2000, Reported at: 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1872. As Revised November 6, 1998. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 9, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2801.
Corrected by, 11/06/1998
Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Halye, 560 Pa. 699, 743 A.2d 916, 1999 Pa. LEXIS 2801 (1999)
Writ of certiorari denied Pennsylvania v. Halye, 529 U.S. 1012, 120 S. Ct. 1287, 146 L. Ed. 2d 233, 2000 U.S. LEXIS 1872 (2000)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Halye v. Pennsylvania, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15106 (M.D. Pa., Feb. 4, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered May 6, 1997 in the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal, No. 2850 of 1996.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part, vacated and remanded in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two counts of involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, two counts of indecent assault, one count of corruption of minors, and one count of endangering the welfare of children, and held that he was a "sexually violent predator" under the Registration of Sexual Offenders Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 9791-9799.6.


OVERVIEW: At the home of relatives, appellant was discovered in a closet with his head near the exposed genitals of a minor. Appellant was convicted and sentenced for involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, indecent assault, corruption of minors, and endangering the welfare of children. The trial court conducted procedures under the Registration of Sexual Offenders Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 9791-9799.6 (the Act), and held that appellant did not rebut the presumption of the Act and was a "sexually violent predator." Appellant sought review. The court reversed the trial court's judgment in part, and vacated and remanded in part. The court concluded that the commonwealth had failed in its burden of proving that appellant was supervising the children at the time of the assault, and accordingly reversed the conviction for endangering the welfare of children. The court found that the provisions of the Act which required an offender to rebut a presumption if he had been convicted of certain enumerated crimes violated the right to due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and that in such cases due process required the prosecution to prove the facts by clear and convincing evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's conviction of appellant for endangering the welfare of children, because the commonwealth had not proved that appellant was supervising the children at the time of the offense, and vacated and remanded the judgment of sentence on his other, sexually-related convictions, because the statute under which appellant had been determined to be a sexually violent predator violated the right to due process.

Porter v. Karivalis


No. 5192 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


718 A.2d 823; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2799


June 23, 1998, Argued  
October 1, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment entered April 7, 1998 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division, No. 92-18520. Before BROWN, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband and wife challenged the constitutionality of the judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Civil Division (Pennsylvania), that awarded to appellee psychologist his fees for services rendered to appellant wife, and found that appellant husband, despite his incarceration, was jointly responsible for the outstanding professional bill under the law of necessaries expressed in 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 4102.


OVERVIEW: Appellant husband and wife sought review of the judgment entered against them for psychological services rendered by appellee psychologist to appellant wife. The trial court determined that appellant husband, despite his incarceration, was jointly responsible for the outstanding professional bill under the law of necessaries expressed in 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 4102. On review, appellants argued that § 4102 violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV because appellant husband did not consent nor have any knowledge of the services rendered to appellant wife, and he was not responsible for the debt given that appellants were separated when appellant wife incurred the debt. The appellate court found that appellant husband did not advance any arguments nor cite any authority to support a showing as to how § 4102 failed to rationally relate to a legitimate state objective, and consequently, found that the issue had been waived. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment because there was no evidence that appellant wife specifically agreed to be solely liable on the debt and the mere fact of appellants' separation did not suspend the duty of spousal support under § 4102.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment in favor of appellee psychologist because the fact that appellant husband and wife were separated when a debt to appellee was incurred did not suspend the duty of spousal support in the statutory law of necessaries, there was no evidence that appellant wife agreed to be solely liable on the debt, and as appellants failed to show a violation of the Due Process Clause, the issue was waived.

Commonwealth v. Nixon


No. 1310 Pittsburgh 1997, No. 1311 Pittsburgh 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


718 A.2d 311; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2858


April 14, 1998, Argued  
September 21, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted October 7, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 3043.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of June 10, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County, Criminal, No. 1260 CR 1996.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of June 10, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County, Criminal, No. 1261 CR 1996. Before CALLAN, J.

This Opinion Substituted by the Court for Withdrawn Opinion of September 21, 1998, Previously reported at: 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2732.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellants of involuntary manslaughter and endangering the welfare of a child and sentenced them to two and a half to five years of incarceration.


OVERVIEW: Appellant parents were convicted of involuntary manslaughter and endangering the welfare of a child. The trial court sentenced appellants to two and a half years to five years of incarceration. Appellants challenged their convictions and sentences, and the court affirmed. The court held that appellants were not denied effective assistance of counsel. The court also held that appellants had a duty to their minor child to override her own religious beliefs and obtain medical treatment for her when her illness became life threatening. There was no conflicted between the Child Protective Services Act, 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6303 (b)(3), and the involuntary manslaughter statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2504. An act that did not qualify as child abuse could still have been done in a manner that caused death and qualified as involuntary manslaughter. The court further held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it refused to instruct the jury on appellants' defense of mistake of fact. Finally, the court held that the sentence ordered was well justified, and was not an abuse of discretion even though it was above the range in the sentencing guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant parents' convictions and sentences for the offenses of involuntary manslaughter and endangering the welfare of a child and held that appellants' counsel was not ineffective, appellants had a duty to obtain medical treatment for their minor child when her condition became life-threatening, the refusal of the trial court to instruct regarding mistake of fact was not an abuse of discretion, and the sentences were valid.

Commonwealth v. Glass


No. 411 Harrisburg 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


718 A.2d 804; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2727



September 15, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, March 19, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Perry County, Criminal Division, No. 296 OF 1996. Before QUIGLEY, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction of possession, possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance, and possession of drug paraphernalia from the Court of Common Pleas of Perry County (Pennsylvania). Appellant challenged his conviction based on whether Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 proscribed the issuance of anticipatory search warrants.


OVERVIEW: A trooper, pursuant to a warrant, found information regarding an individual's drug trafficking activity. The individual chose to cooperate in order to receive more lenient treatment at sentencing, and explained the meaning of his drug trafficking records, which included appellant's name. Based on the information he possessed, the trooper set up a reverse sting operation and obtained an anticipatory search warrant, defined as a warrant based upon an affidavit showing probable cause that at some future time certain evidence of crime would be located at a specified place. Appellant fell for the sting operation, and was arrested. He moved for a motion to suppress based on the alleged unconstitutionality of the anticipatory search warrant. The trial court denied the motion to suppress, and appellant was convicted of possession, possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance, and possession of drug paraphernalia. Appellant argued the constitutionality of the anticipatory search warrant on appeal. The court affirmed concluding that anticipatory warrants passed constitutional muster under Pa. Const. art. I, § 8, so long as they were not executed prematurely.


OUTCOME: Having found no merit to either of appellant's issues, the court affirmed because no prejudicial error in the proceedings before the sentencing court took place, the mandatory minimum provision after conviction and before sentencing was properly invoked, and the court provided appellant with an opportunity to respond.

Commonwealth v. Gullota

SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

August 12, 1998, Filed

DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of insurance fraud.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of insurance fraud under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4117. He challenged his conviction, alleging that § 4117 was unconstitutional. Specifically, he argued that the statute, which provided a financial incentive to the prosecuting attorney for convictions violated due process by undermining the duty of prosecutors as officers of the trial court to seek justice and not just convictions. Appellant also argued that the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict. The court affirmed the conviction and held that the required neutrality that applied to officials performing judicial or quasi-judicial functions was not applicable to prosecutors. The court found that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the conviction. Appellant admitted making false statements to the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation that he could have only made to induce it to grant him a registration sticker for his vehicle. The court found that it strained reason to suggest any purpose for the submission of false insurance information other than to affect the department's determination in approving or disapproving a motor vehicle insurance transaction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of insurance fraud because appellant admitted to the making the false statement, which resulted in his obtaining a vehicle registration sticker for his vehicle, and the statute under which appellant was convicted was constitutional.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Commonwealth sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Snyder County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee driver's motion in limine to prohibit the introduction of appellee's blood alcohol content in his trial for driving with a blood alcohol level greater than 0.10 percent.


OVERVIEW: Appellee driver was found at the scene of a disabled vehicle with a strong odor of alcohol emanating from his breath. Commonwealth established a prima facie case for driving with a blood alcohol level greater than 0.10 percent, and appellee failed to offer any expert testimony to rebut the prima facie case.

The application to supplement the petition for allowance of appeal is granted, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 661.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, August 21, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 529 CRIM 1997.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, August 21, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 528 CRIM 1997.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, August 21, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 530 CRIM 1997.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, August 21, 1997, Docketed September 19, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 527 CRIM 1997.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, August 21, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 531 CRIM 1997.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, August 21, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division at No. 526 CRIM 1997.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, bottle club owner, employees, and dancers, challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted them of violating 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7329(a)'s prohibition against a bottle club offering lewd, immoral, or improper entertainment for profit. Appellants contended that the statute was unconstitutional, and that the evidence was insufficient to convict them.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, bottle club owner, employees, and dancers, were arrested during a raid of the club and charged with violations of Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7329(a), for offering entertainment consisting of conduct prohibited under the statute, i.e., lewd, immoral, or improper entertainment for profit or pecuniary gain. Appellants were convicted and fined. Appellants sought review of their convictions, contending that Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7329 was unconstitutional, and that the evidence was insufficient to convict them. Appellant bottle club owner claimed that he should not have been held accountable for the actions of the dancers, who were not his employees but were independent contractors. On appeal, the court found that because the conduct complained of would be improper whether performed in a licensed establishment, at a charitable function or a "bottle club" regulated by § 7329(a), as applied the statute was not violative of equal protection. Further finding that appellant dancers were employees of appellant bottle club owner because he clearly controlled some of their activities in the club, the court affirmed appellants' convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, finding that because the conduct complained of would be improper wherever it was performed, its prohibition was not violative of equal protection. The court also found that the dancers at the bottle club were employees because the owner received compensation for certain of the dancers' activities and for patrons' access to the establishment.

Commonwealth v. Savich


No. 1805 Pittsburgh 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


716 A.2d 1251; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 867


March 16, 1998, Submitted  
June 15, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 25, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Pa. LEXIS 784.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, July 23, 1997, In the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division at No. 1893 of 1996. Before CONNELLY, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), following defendant's conviction for sexual abuse of children, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6312.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was noticed covertly videotaping children in a changing area of a state park. Defendant was approached by a park ranger and questioned as to his activities, and defendant made inculpatory statements and admitted that nude persons could be seen on his videotape. Defendant was convicted of sexual abuse of children for videotaping nude children for the purpose of sexual stimulation or gratification. Defendant appealed alleging that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, that the physical evidence and his inculpatory statements should have been suppressed, and that his sentencing was improper. On review, the court affirmed. The court noted that statutes were presumed to be constitutional. The court found that a person of common intelligence would grasp that covertly videotaping infant, adolescent and pubescent nude girls was prohibited by 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann § 6312, if the videotapes were made for purposes of sexual gratification or stimulation of the videotaper. The court further held that defendant made his inculpatory statements pursuant to a lawful investigatory stop. Finally, the court found that defendant waived his objections to alleged sentencing errors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence because the court found that defendant, as a person of common intelligence, would understand that the covert taping of nude children for the purposes of the sexual gratification or stimulation of his person was prohibited by the statute. Additionally, the court found that the statements and evidence provided by defendant were made pursuant to a brief and lawful investigatory stop.

Commonwealth v. Hall


Nos. 2791 Philadelphia 1997, 3031 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


713 A.2d 650; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1020


March 19, 1998, Submitted  
June 3, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Petitions for Allowance of Appeal Granted January 6, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Pa. LEXIS 128.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE May 30, 1995 In the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division Berks County, No. 3216/94.

Appeal from the ORDER June 6, 1997 In the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division Berks County, No. 3216/94.

Before KELLER, J.



DISPOSITION: Order granting right to appeal nunc pro tunc affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him and sentenced him to 9 to 23 months after denying his motion to suppress drugs discovered in what he alleged was an illegal search. The commonwealth counter appealed from the trial court's order giving defendant nunc pro tunc appellate rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced to 9 to 23 months after the trial court denied his motion to suppress drugs found during a search by the police. The trial court rejected defendant's Post Conviction Relief Act (P.C.R.A.) petition for being untimely, but the trial court granted defendant leave to appeal nunc pro tunc. Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence and the commonwealth filed a counter appeal challenging the order granting defendant nunc pro tunc appellate rights. The court affirmed the trial court's order granting defendant the right to appeal nunc pro tunc. The court held that even though the P.C.R.A. was intended to be the sole means for seeking to obtain collateral relief after final judgment and direct appeal, there was authority in case law to support granting defendant the right to seek a delinquent appeal right outside of the framework of the P.C.R.A. The court also affirmed the judgment of sentence because the trial court did not err in denying the illegal search suppression order. The court found that defendant's refusal to remove his hand from his pocket gave the officer a reasonable suspicion that he might be armed and justified the pat down search.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting defendant the right to appeal nunc pro tunc because case law provided authority for granting defendant the right to appeal outside of the Post-Conviction Relief Act. The court also affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence because a lawful frisk led to the discovery of drugs in defendant's possession.

Commonwealth v. Coleman


No. 109 Harrisburg 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


713 A.2d 1167; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 839


January 29, 1998, Submitted  
May 28, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE December 31, 1996 Docketed January 02, 1997. In the Court of Common Pleas of LYCOMING County Criminal. Division, No. 95-10054. Before KIESER, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Lycoming County, Pennsylvania, which convicted him for violating 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 4943, pertaining to maximum axle weight.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of exceeding the maximum axle weight upon a highway. Defendant appealed and argued that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 4943 was unconstitutional for vagueness. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that defendant failed to meet the burden of proof as to vagueness. The court concluded that a statute would not be struck down for unconstitutionality unless it clearly and plainly violated the U.S. Constitution and the Pennsylvania Constitution. The court concluded that § 4943 was not unconstitutional because it provided defendant fair notice that the contemplated conduct was forbidden by the statute. In addition, the court held that § 4943 defined the criminal offense with sufficient definiteness and in a manner that did not encourage arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment convicting defendant of exceeding the maximum axle weight. The court held that the applicable statute was constitutional. The court concluded that defendant failed to meet the heavy burden of proving the statute was unconstitutional. The court determined that the statute was clear, specific, definite, and without ambiguity.

Commonwealth v. Montini


No. 1771 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


712 A.2d 761; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 678


November 7, 1997, Submitted  
April 27, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of April 11, 1997 in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal No. 5899/96. Before WEAVER, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania). The trial court convicted defendant of driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a)(1) and driving while his blood alcohol content was above the limit set by 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a)(4).


OVERVIEW: After an officer observed defendant driving erratically, defendant was charged with driving under the influence of alcohol and driving while his blood alcohol content (BAC) was above the statutory limit. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence, and defendant was convicted as charged. The court affirmed. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence of his BAC. The officer had sufficient justification for making an investigatory traffic stop based on how defendant was driving, and the officer had probable cause to arrest defendant when defendant failed four sobriety tests. The trial court did not err in admitting evidence of defendant's BAC to support his conviction under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a)(4). The commonwealth was not required to present expert relation-back testimony in support of this charge because defendant's BAC was significantly higher than the statutory limit, and the time between driving and administering the blood test was relatively short. Evidence of defendant's erratic driving and failed sobriety tests was sufficient to support his conviction under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a)(1).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol and for driving while his blood alcohol content was above the statutory limit. The officer had probable cause to arrest defendant, and there was sufficient evidence to sustain defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol, including defendant's manner of driving, his failed sobriety tests, and his blood alcohol content.

Thomas Assocs. Investigative & Consulting Servs. v. GPI Ltd.


No. 3494 Philadelphia 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


711 A.2d 506; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 642



April 22, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Order entered July 24, 1997 In the Court of Common Pleas of Wayne County, Civil No. 161 JUDGMENT 1997.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation and individual sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Wayne County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their petition to strike or open a confession of judgment in favor of appellee consultant. Appellee obtained a confessed judgment against appellants and caused the judgment to be transferred to another county.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation and individual challenged a trial court order, which dismissed their petition to strike or open a confession of judgment in favor of appellee consultant. Appellee obtained a confessed judgment against appellants in the amount of $ 12,845.91. Appellants' counsel was mailed a copy of the judgment and notice that the judgment was entered on November 5, 1996, pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 236. Appellee filed for a writ of execution instructing the sheriff to execute on appellants' property in order to collect the judgment. In March 1997, appellants filed a petition to strike the confessed judgment. In May 1997, appellee filed a notice of judgment and execution. In June 1997, appellants filed a second petition. On appeal, the court held that the trial court erred in finding that judgment notice issued under Pa. R. Civ. P. 236, and was sufficient to run the timeliness clock. The court found that Pa. R. Civ. P. 2956.1(c)(2) required appellee to serve notice on appellants of intention to execute on appellants' property. Accordingly, appellants had 30 days from service of the execution notice on May 19, 1997, to file their petition. The court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded, holding that under the revised confession of judgment rules, the petition to strike or open a confession of judgment filed by appellant corporation and individual was timely filed.

Commonwealth v. Hudak


No. 374 Pittsburgh 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


710 A.2d 1213; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 544


October 28, 1997, Argued  
March 30, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence, January 13, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division at No. CC9604596. Before O'TOOLE, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence is reversed. Case remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), convicting defendant of removal or falsification of identification number, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7102(b), dealing in vehicles with removed or falsified numbers, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7103(b), and receiving stolen property, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3925(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of removal or falsification of identification number, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7102(b), dealing in vehicles with removed or falsified numbers, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7103(b), and receiving stolen property, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3925(a), following a search of his auto body shop. The police did not have a search warrant, but instead told defendant that they were searching the premises under the authority of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6803(c), which allows for administrative searches of auto body shops. However, the police informed defendant that they had received a tip that he was selling stolen parts and that they were there to check vehicle information. The court reversed, finding that the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress, because the search violated defendant's U.S. Const. Amend. IV rights. The court held that the police could not use their authority to perform administrative searches of auto body shops under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6803(c) as a pretext to gather evidence of criminal activity, that the search was not a routine inspection, and that the Fourth Amendment warrant requirements applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of sentence, because the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence where the evidence was obtained through an illegal search.

Affirmed by Commonwealth v. Cotto, 562 Pa. 32, 753 A.2d 217, 2000 Pa. LEXIS 1522 (2000)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE May 19, 1997 In the Court of Common Pleas of LANCASTER County, CRIMINAL No. 1878-1996; 3167-1996. Before ECKMAN, J.
Commonwealth v. Cotto, 37 Pa. D. & C.4th 396, 1997 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 54 (1997)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) that convicted appellant of six counts of armed robbery and two counts of criminal conspiracy. Appellant argued that the court erred when it held that the 1995 amendments to the Juvenile Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6301 et seq., as amended were neither unconstitutionally vague and nor unconstitutional.

The court held that the Juvenile Act was not unconstitutionally vague becauise it provided adequate notice of the meaning of "a public interest" and sufficiently guided a court presented with a question of transfer. The court also held that the shifting of the burden to change venue to juvenile court to appellant was constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Davis


No. 900 Pittsburgh, 1997


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


708 A.2d 116; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 29


November 10, 1997, Submitted  
February 2, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Application for Reargument Denied April 14, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order Dated April 9, 1997, in the Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County, Criminal Division, at No. 401 & 402 CRIM 1996. Before NICKLEACH, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed without prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which denied without prejudice defendant's motion for extraordinary relief that challenged the constitutionality of the sexually violent predator provisions of Pennsylvania's Megan's Law, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9791 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with eight counts of involuntary deviate sexual intercourse committed on three boys under the age of 11, and pleaded guilty to four of the counts. Defendant challenged Pennsylvania's Megan's Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9791 et seq., that required him to register as a sex offender, and automatically presumed that he was a sexually violent predator. A hearing was scheduled to allow defendant to rebut the statutory presumption. The trial court denied defendant's a motion for extraordinary relief that challenged Megan's Law and requested a bar to a second prosecution for the same offense. Defendant's application to enforce automatic stay was granted before the contested hearing took place. On appeal, the court affirmed the order denying extraordinary relief, and held that the adjudicatory hearing to overcome the presumption was not an unconstitutional second trial in violation of the double jeopardy clause, that there was no evidentiary hearing requirement for presentencing motions for extraordinary relief, and there was no requirement to prove that defendant was a sexually violent predator in order to prove involuntary deviate sexual intercourse with a minor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying defendant's written motion for extraordinary relief without a hearing because no hearing was required under the rules of criminal procedure. There was a presumption under Pennsylvania's Megan's Law that appellant was a sexually violent predator because the victim of the offense was a minor, and the hearing to dispute that presumption was not a second trial in violation of the double jeopardy clause.

Commonwealth v. Bell

SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


706 A.2d 855; 1998 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9


July 28, 1997, Submitted  
January 13, 1998, Filed
