Amicone v. Shoaf


NO. 00610 PITTSBURGH 1992


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


423 Pa. Super. 281; 620 A.2d 1222; 1993 Pa. Super. LEXIS 688


December 3, 1992, Argued  
February 26, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order March 30, 1992 In the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County Civil Division, No. 9444 of 1988. Before MCCORMICK, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant customer challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania) denying appellant's motions for posttrial relief and for removal of the compulsory nonsuit entered against him in his action for malicious prosecution against appellee restaurant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant customer ordered breakfast at appellee restaurant. Appellant was dissatisfied with the state of the sausage and returned it to the kitchen. He then disputed the amount of the bill, left a portion of the outstanding balance, and departed. Appellee assistant manager contacted the police department, which sent an officer to investigate. When appellant refused to pay the balance due he was charged with theft of services, a summary offense that the district justice dismissed. Appellant then filed a civil action for malicious prosecution. At the close of appellant's case, the trial court granted appellees' motion for nonsuit. The court held that in order to prevail on a claim of malicious prosecution, plaintiff must first show that defendant initiated criminal proceedings against plaintiff without probable cause. In affirming the nonsuit, the court ruled that the trial court correctly found that the evidence was sufficient to establish probable cause based on appellant's refusal to pay the amount listed for his breakfast, thereby justifying appellees' initiation of criminal proceedings against appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant customer's motions for removal of the compulsory nonsuit entered against him and for posttrial relief in his suit for malicious prosecution against appellee restaurant. The court held that the trial court correctly found the evidence sufficient to establish probable cause, thereby justifying appellees' initiation of criminal proceedings against appellant.

Commonwealth v. Harris


No. 02521 Philadelphia 1991, No. 02522 Philadelphia 1991, No. 02523 Philadelphia 1991, No. 02021 Philadelphia 1991, No. 02524 Philadelphia 1991, No. 02770 Philadelphia 1991, No. 02773 Philadelphia 1991


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


423 Pa. Super. 190; 620 A.2d 1175; 1993 Pa. Super. LEXIS 639


September 17, 1992, Argued  
February 19, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 8902-2483; 2483 1/2.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 9004-2961.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 9001-2048-53.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 9001-2053.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 8702-1619.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 9007-295, 298, 299.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at No. 9007-2514 & 2516. Before TEMIN, J.



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed. The matters are remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted parole, aggregating the sentences imposed upon several appellees. Appellant contended that the trial court was without authority to grant early parole, under the proper interpretation of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9757 (1982), 61 P.S. §§ 314, 331.17.


OVERVIEW: In several cases, the trial court granted parole and aggregated several appellees' sentences. Appellant state challenged the orders, and the court reversed and remanded, because the trial court was without authority to grant early parole, under the proper interpretation of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9757 (1982), 61 P.S. §§ 314, 331.17. The court held that once another judge sentenced appellees on another conviction, or a "back-time" sentence was imposed due to a parole violation, that original sentencing court could not expect to continue to exercise parole power. The commission of subsequent crimes frustrated the original attempt of the court to rehabilitate the defendant. The court noted that an order by the sentencing judge which purported to either grant parole to a person serving a maximum sentence in excess of two years, or to deny parole to such a person, was beyond the authority of the sentencing court and was, therefore, a nullity. The court found that there was absolutely no basis in the law for applying § 9757 so as to aggregate the "back-time" part of a county prison sentence with a subsequently imposed sentence so as to change the jurisdiction over re-parole.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded orders granting parole and aggregating several appellees' sentences because the trial court was without authority to grant early parole. The court held that once another judge sentenced appellees on another conviction, or a "back-time" sentence was imposed due to a parole violation, that original sentencing court could not expect to continue to exercise parole power.

Hill v. Divecchio


No. 522 Pittsburgh 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


425 Pa. Super. 355; 625 A.2d 642; 1993 Pa. Super. LEXIS 587


December 12, 1991, Argued  
February 12, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument or Reconsideration Denied April 26, 1993. Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied June 20, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order entered February 20, 1991, Docketed February 21, 1991, in the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Civil, No. 2264-A-1985. Before DOMITROVICH, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which awarded appellees, natural grandmother and step-grandfather, visitation rights to appellant's child. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in awarding visitation rights to appellees, because they lacked standing under the Grandparents Visitation Act, 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5312.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother lived with her mother and step-father until she married. After appellant's child was born, she and the father later obtained a divorce. Appellant and the child moved back to the house of appellee natural grandmother and step-grandfather, and later moved out. For over two years, appellees had little contact with the child. Then, appellees filed a petition, in a custody action commenced by the father, seeking grand-parental partial custody. The trial court issued an order adopting the custody counselor's recommendations that appellees be awarded partial physical custody of the child. The father's custody rights were not disputed. Appellant sought review, and the court affirmed the trial court's award of visitation rights to appellee natural grandmother, but reversed the order as to appellee step-grandfather, because the former had standing to bring an action, but the latter did not. The court held that the plain language of the Grandparents Visitation Act, 23 Pa. Const. Stat. Ann. § 5312 provided for application by a grandparent of the party, and appellee step-grandfather was not included in this definition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order awarding visitation rights in favor of appellee natural grandmother, but reversed the order as it pertained to appellee step-grandfather, because the former had standing to bring an action, but the latter lack such standing. The court held that the plain language of the statute provided for application by a grandparent of the party, and appellee step-grandfather was not included in this definition.

Commonwealth v. Stipetich


No. 1773 Pittsburgh, 1991, No. 1801 Pittsburgh, 1991


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


423 Pa. Super. 427; 621 A.2d 606; 1993 Pa. Super. LEXIS 549


December 4, 1992, Argued  
February 9, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Granted September 21, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order entered October 8, 1991, Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Criminal Division at No. 665 Miscellaneous Docket, August 1991.

Appeal from the Order entered October 8, 1991, Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Criminal Division at No. 666 Miscellaneous Docket, August 1991. Before DAUER, J.



DISPOSITION: Order of October 8, 1991, Dismissing the Criminal Complaints is Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a judgment by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed criminal complaints where a non-prosecution agreement was entered between appellees and the police department and full performance was rendered by appellees.


OVERVIEW: Appellees' attorney and a sergeant reached an agreement about appellees' case. The agreement was immediately fulfilled by appellee husband's answering all questions asked of him by the police officers. Police later filed criminal complaints against appellees in disregard of the agreement. Counsel filed a motion to dismiss the charges against appellees based on the non-prosecution agreement. The trial court granted the motion and dismissed the charges. Upon appeal, the court affirmed the dismissal and concluded that the record supported the trial court's finding that a binding agreement had been entered by appellees and the police, and therefore, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in enforcing it. Appellees, in reliance on the agreement, divulged all information that the police requested, and during this process gave up the valued constitutional guarantee against self-incrimination. While the police, rather than the prosecutor, entered into this agreement, both appellees and their attorney were induced to believe that the police had full negotiating authority over the case and they detrimentally relied on that belief and the bargain that had been struck.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the criminal complaints against appellees and held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in enforcing the non-prosecution agreement between appellees and the police because appellees entered and fulfilled a valid non-prosecution agreement.

Commonwealth v. Arriaga


NO. 00638 PHILADELPHIA 1992


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


422 Pa. Super. 52; 618 A.2d 1011; 1993 Pa. Super. LEXIS 9


October 5, 1992, Submitted  
January 7, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence January 28, 1992, In the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, CRIMINAL, No. 2810 and 2811, APRIL TERM, 1989. Before COHEN, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of delivery of a controlled substance and criminal conspiracy. Defendant challenged his sentence, which was imposed pursuant to the provision for violation of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508(a)(3)(i).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged and convicted of delivery of a controlled substance and criminal conspiracy. The court imposed an enhanced prison sentence as a result of defendant's previous convictions, under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508(a)(3)(i). Defendant appealed, claiming that § 7508(a)(3)(i) violated the double jeopardy clause, U.S. Const. amend. V, and the due process clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, holding that defendant's sentence was not a reprosecution and was a punishment of only his third offense. The court held therefore, that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508 was proper in that its operation did not constitute double jeopardy and did not violate due process. Therefore, the court affirmed the conviction and sentence as imposed by the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for delivery of a controlled substance and criminal conspiracy and his sentence imposed therefrom. The court held that the enhancement provision from which defendant's prison sentence was issued, as a result of his previous convictions, did not violate the double jeopardy clause or the due process clause.

Ottavio v. Fibreboard Corp.


No. 3421 Philadelphia, 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


421 Pa. Super. 284; 617 A.2d 1296; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4263


May 1, 1992, Argued  
December 16, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Name of this CASE has been changed by the Court February 16, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County, No. 1622 (1409) July Term, 1982.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for a new trial. Jurisdiction is not retained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant manufacturer of asbestos products sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which entered judgment against appellant in the amount of $ 18,795, in appellee injured party's action against numerous manufacturers for injuries allegedly sustained as a result of workplace exposure to asbestos.


OVERVIEW: The lower court entered judgment against appellant manufacturer of asbestos products in the amount of $ 18,795, in appellee injured party's action against numerous manufacturers for injuries resulting from workplace exposure to asbestos. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for a new trial because appellant was entitled to a jury instruction that appellee had the right to bring a second action if he developed future asbestos related injury. The court held that the evidence was sufficient because appellee established that he worked in the vicinity of the use of asbestos products manufactured by appellant. Because appellant waived its right to a 12 person jury under Pa. Const. art. I, § 6, it consented to the procedure established by Phila., Pa., R. Civ. P. 1007.2 allowing trial to continue so long as at least 6 jurors remained in service. Thus, it was not error for the trial court to proceed with seven jurors after one of the original eight jurors was excused because of illness. The trial court did not err in refusing to submit to the jury a verdict sheet including the bankrupt manufacturers of asbestos products based on the automatic stay provision of the Bankruptcy Code.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for a new trial in appellee injured party's action for injures resulting from workplace exposure to asbestos because appellant manufacturer of asbestos products was entitled to a jury instruction that appellee had the right to bring a second action if he developed future asbestos related injury.

Commonwealth v. Pestinikas


No. 375 Philadelphia, 1989, No. 395 Philadelphia, 1989


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


421 Pa. Super. 371; 617 A.2d 1339; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4260


September 12, 1991, Argued  
December 10, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Lackawanna County, No. 85-CR-1020-A-E.

Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Lackawanna County, No. 85-CR-1019-A-D



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania) where a jury found defendants guilty of murder of the third degree in connection with the starvation and dehydration death of 92 year old man who lived with defendants.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed their conviction for murder of the third degree. On appeal, the court affirmed the verdict and held that there was evidence that the victim's death had been caused by defendants' failure to provide the food and medical care as they agreed to do by oral contract. The court held that the evidence was sufficient for the jury to determine that this contract existed between the victim and defendants. The court held that the evidence did show that defendants' failure to provide the victim with food and medical care was willful and malicious. The court held that the trial court did not err in allowing the jury to see photographs depicting the decedent's nude body because its essential evidentiary value was greater that the likelihood of its inflaming the jury. The court held that testimony that defendants exerted political influence on the prosecuting attorney to avoid prosecution was not admitted in error because it tended to show consciousness of guilt. The court held that defendants' statement to police was correctly admitted because it was not coerced and they were not subjected to custodial interrogation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' conviction in the trial court because the evidence was sufficient to show that a contract existed between defendants and the victim, which obligated defendants to provide food and medical care for victim and his death was caused by defendants comply with the contract. The defendants' had a duty to act under the contract, and their omission was sufficient for a conviction for murder of the third degree.

Fornwalt v. Follmer

420 Pa. Super. 413; 616 A.2d 1040; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4006


September 17, 1992, Argued  
November 24, 1992, Filed

DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County (Pennsylvania), which held that appellee mother's claim was not barred by the principle of res judicata and ordered that appellant pay child support for his son.


OVERVIEW: Appellee mother filed a child support action against appellant father, and the action was dismissed because it was barred by the six-year statute of limitations. Later the statute was struck down as unconstitutional, and the legislature enacted an 18-year statute of limitations for such actions. Appellee filed her action again, and the lower court ordered that the appellant pay child support. The court affirmed the order of the lower court, holding that because the six-year statute of limitations was unconstitutional it was void ab initio, and the previous judgment based on the unconstitutional statute had no res judicata effect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court, holding that res judicata did not apply because the statute of limitations that barred appellee mother's claim the first time had been declared unconstitutional and was therefore void ab initio.

Commonwealth v. Johnson


NO. 3140 PHILADELPHIA 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


421 Pa. Super. 433; 618 A.2d 415; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3880


September 16, 1992, Argued  
November 16, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered September 25, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 9008-2730. Before GEISZ, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of knowing or intentional possession of a controlled substance, possession of a controlled substance with intent to manufacture or deliver, and sentenced him under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of knowing or intentional possession of a controlled substance, possession of a controlled substance with intent to manufacture or deliver, and sentenced under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508. The court affirmed, holding that the evidence was sufficient because an officer saw a bag in defendant's hand for a brief instant before watching him hide it in a tire. Money and cocaine were found in the bag, and the jury could infer defendant's control of the bag because he hid it. The court found no abuse of discretion in the trial court's refusal of the jury's request to re-read the officer's testimony, or in giving an instruction concerning the legal definition of possession instead, because the instruction was given in accordance with the standard instruction and took into account the testimonies of the officer and defendant. The court found that § 7508 was not "cruel and unusual" on its face because the sentence and fine were proportionate to the gravity of the offense. The court held that the issue of whether it was unconstitutional as applied was not before the court because payment would not become due until defendant was paroled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence and convictions for knowing or intentional possession of a controlled substance and possession of a controlled substance with intent to manufacture or deliver, holding that the evidence was sufficient because an officer saw a bag in defendant's hand for an instant, watched him hide it in a tire, and found money and cocaine in the bag.

In re Baby Boy S.

SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order Entered October 4, 1991, in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Orphans Court, at No. 157 of 1989. Before O'MALLEY, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Orphans Court, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), terminating her parental rights to her three-year-old son, arguing that sufficient grounds did not exist to terminate her parental rights under 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2511(a)(2).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was a teenage unwed mother who grew up in a dysfunctional family. She agreed to give her son up for adoption, later retracted, and the adopting parties filed a complaint for custody, and petitioned to terminate appellant's parental rights. The trial court entered a decree that terminated appellant's parental rights to her three-year-old son. The court held that sufficient grounds existed to terminate appellant's parental rights under 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2511(a)(2). The statutory criteria necessary for the termination of appellant's parental rights were met, and the best interests of the child lay with the adoptive parents. Any other result would have placed the child's life in real jeopardy. Appellant was unable to take care of the child, and had left the jurisdiction without giving notice. Moreover, the father had admitted to prior problems with drugs and alcohol.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that terminated appellant's parental rights to her three-year-old son because sufficient grounds existed under the applicable statute. The best interest of the child lay with the adoptive parents. Any other result would have placed the child's life in real jeopardy.

Commonwealth v. Hanawalt


No. 02360 Philadelphia 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


419 Pa. Super. 411; 615 A.2d 432; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3728


June 17, 1992, Argued  
October 27, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected January 5, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order entered in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Luzerne County at No. 1445 of 1990 & 1081 of 1990. Before BROMINSKI, J.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated and case remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), denying his motion in limine to preclude the commonwealth from introducing the statements of his four-year-old daughter in his prosecution for rape, indecent assault and incest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with rape, statutory rape, indecent assault and incest, which related to his four-year-old daughter. Appellant filed a motion in limine to preclude the commonwealth from introducing the statements of the child. Following an in-camera examination of the child, the trial court determined that she was incompetent to testify and, therefore, unavailable as a witness. The court denied appellant's motion in limine and allowed the commonwealth to introduce the child's hearsay statements under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5985.1. Appellant took an immediate appeal from the order. The court held that § 5985.1 did not violate appellant's right to confront and cross-examine witnesses under the Pennsylvania and United States Constitutions. Therefore, the court vacated the order of the trial court, and remanded the case in order for the court to make further factual determinations.


OUTCOME: The order denying appellant's motion in limine was vacated and remanded for further factual determinations in appellant's prosecution for rape, assault and incest. The applicable statute did not violate appellant's state and federal constitutional right to confront and cross-examine witnesses.

Commonwealth v. Deer


NO. 1515 PITTSBURGH 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


419 Pa. Super. 321; 615 A.2d 386; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3608


June 17, 1992, Submitted  
October 21, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied April 5, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered August 2, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, at No. CC 8906186. Before ROSS, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant business owner challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) finding him guilty of unlawfully obtaining gas for his place of business by an illegal tap.


OVERVIEW: Appellant business owner leased a building in the early 1980's wherein gas service was terminated in 1983. Gas company surveillance disclosed vapors emanating from a flue on three occasions, whereupon inspection led to the discovery of two overhead gas furnaces on the property and an unauthorized connection to a main gas line in front of the building. A gas line was installed inside the premises to carry gas to the furnace. During this period appellant had exclusive control of the premises. At trial, appellant was convicted in a non-jury proceeding of one count of theft of services in violation of a local statute. On appeal, appellant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence, and a statutory presumption in the local law that a tampered measuring device created an inference that a person intended to receive utility service without compensation. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding the statute constitutional because it recognized that more that one person might be the beneficiary of service, and the evidence sufficient because of appellant's exclusive control of the premises, operation of a business thereon, and regular presence at the building.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment against appellant business owner for theft of gas service; a statutory presumption was constitutional and evidence that appellant had exclusive control of the premises, operated a business thereon, and was regularly present there was sufficient to sustain a conviction.

Commonwealth v. Greenich


No. 00902 Pittsburgh, 1991, No. 00903 Pittsburgh, 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


420 Pa. Super. 551; 617 A.2d 323; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3609


March 18, 1992, Argued  
October 21, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 4, 1993. Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied May 5, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Erie County, Criminal Division, at No. 1809 of 1990.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Erie County, Criminal Division, at No. 1690 of 1990. Before BOZZA, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Erie County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) after they were convicted of obscene and other sexual materials and performances, under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903, on the ground, inter alia, that the trial court erred in failing to allow appellants' sociologist to give expert testimony regarding contemporary community standards in Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: Appellants managed stores that sold magazines depicting sadomasochistic behavior, homosexuality, and males ejaculating. Appellants were tried for obscene and other sexual materials and performances, under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903. At trial, an officer testified that she was working with the State Police Organized Crime Unit (Pennsylvania) at the time of the arrest. The trial court instructed the jury that there was no relationship between the unit and the case. After an in camera review, the trial court did not let appellants' sociologist testify; he would have testified with regard to which magazines were acceptable to the average Pennsylvania adult. Appellants were convicted. The court affirmed the convictions, holding that the trial court did not err in failing to grant a mistrial, as the instruction that the case was not related to the organized crime unit would have dispelled any mistaken impressions reached by the jury, and that the trial court did not err in failing to allow the sociologist to testify, as his testimony would have provided little assistance to the jury in regard to the acceptance of the material by Pennsylvania residents.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the trial court properly refused to allow appellants' sociologist to testify because his testimony regarding what magazines were acceptable to the average Pennsylvania adult would have provided little assistance to the jury in determining obscenity; the instruction that there was no connection between the organized crime unit and the case cured any misconceptions the jury may have had.

Commonwealth v. Clutter


NO. 103 PITTSBURGH 1992


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


419 Pa. Super. 275; 615 A.2d 362; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3599


August 17, 1992, Submitted  
October 20, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied October 5, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered December 18, 1991, in the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, at No. 181 Misc. 91. Before SCHERER, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus, finding that appellant waived extradition as a condition of his probation with Maryland.


OVERVIEW: A detainer was lodged against appellant for violation of his probation with Maryland. Appellant requested extradition, under the Uniform Interstate Agreement on Detainers, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9101 et seq. When appellant was not extradited, he filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The trial court denied the writ, finding that appellant had waived extradition as a condition of his probation in Maryland. Appellant argued that his institutional liberties were being restricted in violation of his due process rights, under the U.S. Constitution and Pa. Const. art. I, § 11, and that his counsel was ineffective. The court affirmed the judgment on a different basis, holding that appellant's due process rights were not violated, as the ultimate consequence would be that the detainer based on the violation of probation charge would be replaced with a detainer on the re-imposed sentence, which would result in similar adverse effects on appellant's institutional treatment. Accordingly, appellant's counsel was not ineffective for failing to make the due process argument.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that appellant was not entitled to relief in the form of a writ of habeas corpus because the detainer lodged against him for violating his probation with Maryland did not violate his due process rights. The ultimate consequence of extraditing appellant to satisfy the detainer would be the replacement of the probation detainer with a detainer for the re-imposed underlying sentence.

Commonwealth v. Vergotz


NO. 1815 PITTSBURGH 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


420 Pa. Super. 440; 616 A.2d 1379; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3273


June 16, 1992, Argued  
September 28, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied December 4, 1992. As Revised February 10, 1993. As Revised February 17, 1993. Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied April 20, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered October 15, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County, Criminal Division, at No. 1491 of 1990. Before CONNELLY, J.



DISPOSITION: Convictions for summary offenses vacated; in all other aspects, the judgment of sentence is affirmed. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him for deceptive business practices, sentenced him to prison, and imposed fines.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who operated an auto inspection station, was charged with deceptive business practices in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4107(a) and issuing certificates of inspection in violation of 5 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4730(a)(2). Appellant's challenge claimed that physical evidence was improperly seized at his business without probable cause; the action under § 4730 was not commenced within the time required by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5553(a); the law did not permit prosecution under both a general provision and special provision prohibiting the same type of conduct; and definitions in § 4107(a)(4) were unconstitutionally vague. The court vacated the summary offenses under § 4730, but otherwise affirmed. There was probable cause for an officer to search appellant's business establishment, so admission of evidence seized was proper. Appellant could be prosecuted under both the general provision in § 4107 and special provisions in 4730(a), as the former contained elements not in the latter. However, conviction was reversed as to summary offenses under 4730 for failure to prosecute within 30 days of discovery, as required by § 5553(a). Finally, § 4107 was not void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellant's convictions for summary offenses where the charges were not brought within 30 days of discovery, as required by statute. The court otherwise affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for deceptive business practices, finding that the statute invoked was constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Yount


NO. 01969 PITTSBURGH 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


419 Pa. Super. 613; 615 A.2d 1316; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3175


June 29, 1992, Submitted  
September 21, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied November 23, 1992. Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied July 29, 1993. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 23, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Pa. LEXIS 701.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Post Conviction Relief Act September 6, 1991. In the Court of Common Pleas of Clearfield County. Criminal Division, No. 2 May Session 1966



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, after numerous proceedings subsequent to his 1966 conviction for first degree murder and rape, challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Clearfield County, Criminal Division, No. 2 (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his third petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a lower court's dismissal of his third petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act. Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and rape and sentenced to life imprisonment. Subsequent to his conviction, defendant initiated numerous post conviction proceedings. The court affirmed the lower court's dismissal of defendant's third petition for relief because it determined that under the applicable statute, 18 P.S. § 4701, repealed and recodified at 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1102, under the clear wording of the statute, the sentencing court could not sentence a first degree murderer to a lesser term. The court found that the applicable statute clearly and unambiguously required the sentencing court to sentence defendant to life imprisonment, notwithstanding evidence of aggravating or mitigating circumstances. The court further determined that defendant had not been denied his right to equal protection under the law because all offenders in noncapital first-degree murder cases were given the same punishment: the removal of the offender from society for the remainder of his life.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court which dismissed defendant's third petition for post conviction relief from his sentence to life imprisonment for first degree murder and rape because it determined that under the applicable statute the sentencing court could not sentence a first degree murderer to a lesser term and he had not been denied equal protection.

Commonwealth v. Cottam


NO. 02319 PHL 91


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


420 Pa. Super. 311; 616 A.2d 988; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3142


May 27, 1992, Argued  
September 17, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied November 24, 1992. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 30, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of July 19, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal Division, at No. 23 and 24 of 1989. Before CAPPELLINI, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged their conviction from the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), of murder in the third degree, recklessly endangering another person (two counts), and endangering the welfare of children (two counts), all charges stemming from the starvation death of their 14-year-old son, and the malnutrition of their 12-year-old daughter.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted appellant parents of murder in the third degree, recklessly endangering another person, and endangering the welfare of children. All charges stemmed from the starvation death of their 14-year-old son, and the malnutrition of their 12-year-old daughter. On appeal, appellants contended that the trial court erred in permitting the prosecution to ask questions during voir dire which challenged appellants' religious beliefs on tithing. The court disagreed, finding probing necessary in order to determine whether a prospective juror was predisposed to disregard any jury instruction pertaining to potential defenses. Appellants further argued that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury that if the jury found the children to be of sufficient intellect and maturity and to have voluntarily refrained from eating based on their religious beliefs, then they must conclude that appellants did not have a legal duty to provide food. The court held that even if the children were considered mature enough to freely exercise their religious beliefs, this did not dispel appellants' duty to provide them with parental care, direction and sustenance. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant parents' conviction on the grounds that there was sufficient evidence to support the verdict and the prosecution sustained its burden of proving each element of the crimes for which appellants were charged.

Commonwealth v. Gipple


No. 694 Harrisburg 1990, No. 695 Harrisburg 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


418 Pa. Super. 119; 613 A.2d 600; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3103


June 3, 1992, Argued  
September 15, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised October 20, 1992. Second Revision November 12, 1992. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 21, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence October 03, 1990, Docketed October 04, 1990, in the Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County, Criminal, No. 200 of 1990 C.A.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence October 03, 1990, Docketed October 09, 1990, in the Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County, Criminal, No. 140 of 1990



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Huntingdon County (Pennsylvania) after he entered a guilty plea to possession with intent to deliver cocaine, possession with intent to deliver marijuana and hashish, and conspiracy. He contended that the mandatory imposition of fines under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7508, regardless of his ability to pay, violated the excessive fines provision of the Pa. Const. art. I, § 13.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered a guilty plea to possession with intent to deliver cocaine, possession with intent to deliver marijuana and hashish, and conspiracy. He was sentenced to jail and he was given a mandatory fine under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7508. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of § 7508 contending that the mandatory imposition of fines regardless of a person's ability to pay violated the excessive fines provision of Pa. Const. art. I, § 13. The court disagreed and affirmed. The mere fact that the trial court did not inquire into appellant's ability to pay was irrelevant to the question of whether the fine was excessive. The proper question for a violation of art. I, § 13 was whether the fine was irrational or unreasonable and there was nothing within the legislature's decision to impose a mandatory fine that was irrational or unreasonable. There was no evidence to suggest that art. I, § 13 was offended when those properly and justly convicted of drug dealing were sentenced to pay for the price they cost society.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for possession with intent to deliver cocaine, possession with intent to deliver marijuana and hashish, and conspiracy because the mandatory imposition of fines was neither irrational nor unreasonable. Therefore, it did not violate the excessive fines provision of the Pennsylvania Constitution.

Commonwealth v. Fouse

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Entered November 19, 1991, in the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, at No. 1659 C 1990.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), convicting him of incest under Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4302, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, corruption of minors, and indecent assault.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of incest, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, corruption of minors, and indecent assault, and sought review. The court found that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague and that appellant had fair notice that his acts violated the incest statute. The court found that neither the errors committed during trial nor the cumulative effect of those asserted errors warranted a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction, finding that the incest statute was not unconstitutionally vague and applied to acts of intercourse between a father and his sons, and that errors committed during the trial did not cumulatively warrant a new trial.

Heard v. Heard


NO. 01779 PITTSBURGH 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


418 Pa. Super. 250; 614 A.2d 255; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2664


December 11, 1991, Argued  
August 20, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered September 26, 1990 In the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County Family Division, No. FD90-7400



DISPOSITION: Order vacated as to any finding or conclusion regarding protection from wife's abuse and affirmed in all other respects. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Family Division (Pennsylvania), which denied her post trial motions following an order granting a protection of abuse order pursuant to the Protection From Abuse Act, 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6101 et seq., against both appellant and appellee husband.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife and appellee husband were involved in an altercation that resulted in both parties seeking relief pursuant to the Protection From Abuse Act (PFA Act), 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6101 et seq. A hearing was scheduled on appellant's petition within the 10-day limitations period set forth in 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6107(a); however, a hearing was not scheduled on appellee's petition within the required time period. The trial court granted protection from abuse orders against both appellant and appellee. When appellant's post trial motions were denied, she sought review. The court held that the trial court had no authority to grant appellee relief under the PFA Act because no hearing was set within the mandatory 10-day window of § 6107(a). The trial court did not abuse its discretion by issuing an exclusion order of only two-weeks' duration against appellee because the trial court could have concluded from the conflicting testimony given by appellant and appellee that two weeks was sufficient. The court vacated that portion of the order pertaining to appellee's protection from appellant's abuse and affirmed the remainder of the order.


OUTCOME: The portion of the order regarding appellee husband's protection from appellant wife's abuse was vacated because the trial court lacked authority to grant him relief under the Protection From Abuse Act as no hearing was set on his petition within the mandatory 10-day period. The court affirmed the remainder of the order as the trial court did not abuse its discretion by issuing an exclusion order of only two-weeks' duration against appellee.

Commonwealth v. Anthony


No. 01155 Philadelphia 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


418 Pa. Super. 82; 613 A.2d 581; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2613


March 25, 1992, Argued  
August 18, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered March 23, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal, No. M.R. No. 88-913315.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded for proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth, in a motion for reconsideration, challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that found itself bound by operation of the doctrine of collateral estoppel to the previous order granting suppression in the municipal court and ordered the property seized from appellee involving an illegal gambling operation investigation returned to him.


OVERVIEW: Items seized from appellee's home were suppressed by the Philadelphia municipal court that determined the warrant application failed to establish probable cause pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 324. Appellee filed a petition for the return of the seized items. The commonwealth challenged the petition pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 342(b) on the grounds items were contraband. The trial court ordered seized items returned to appellant holding it was bound by the doctrine of collateral estoppel to previous order granting suppression in the municipal court. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not have equal but appellate jurisdiction, which required it to review the municipal court's suppression motion as if it were anew. The case was reversed and remanded for a return of property hearing pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 324.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment finding that appellee's motion for return of property was granted in error because the trial court was not bound by collateral estoppel as it was authorized to review the municipal court's findings with appellate jurisdiction.

Commonwealth v. Thill


No. 1964 Pittsburgh, 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


417 Pa. Super. 485; 612 A.2d 1043; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2532


June 1, 1992, Argued  
August 6, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied April 29, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered November 16, 1990, Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Criminal Division at No. CC9000740.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Sentence Reversed; Case Remanded; Jurisdiction Relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment entered by the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of operating a vehicle while under the influence of alcohol to a degree which rendered him incapable of safe driving, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(1), and operating a vehicle while the amount of alcohol by weight in his blood was .10 percent or greater, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(4).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with operating a vehicle while under the influence of alcohol to a degree which rendered him incapable of safe driving, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(1), and operating a vehicle while the amount of alcohol by weight in his blood was .10 percent or greater, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(4). The suppression court denied appellant's pre-trial motion to suppress the test results on the basis that the solution used to calibrate the breath testing device, and to test it for accuracy, was not produced in compliance with the applicable statute and regulation: 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(c)(1) and 67 Pa. Code. § 74.24(d). The court reversed the judgment of sentence because it determined that the suppression court properly concluded that the Commonwealth had not overcome its burden of demonstrating that the Toxicology Section and the Environmental Chemistry Section of the Department of Laboratories for the county were independent laboratories within the meaning of 67 Pa. Code § 77.24 and thus were not within the regulation. However, the court found that appellant was entitled to a new trial at which the results of the breathalyzer test were inadmissible.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence and remanded the case because it determined that appellant was entitled to a new trial because the results of a breathalyzer test of appellant's blood alcohol level, using simulator solution which was not produced and tested in independent laboratories, was in violation of regulatory requirements and was inadmissible.

Commonwealth v. Amundsen


No. 01884 Pittsburgh 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


416 Pa. Super. 464; 611 A.2d 309; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2482



July 31, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence October 03, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County, Criminal, No. 665 Criminal 1990.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas, Mercer County (Pennsylvania), which entered a judgment of sentence after appellant pleaded guilty to two counts of homicide by motor vehicle related to driving while under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3735.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded guilty to two counts of homicide by motor vehicle related to driving while under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3735. The trial court entered a judgment of sentence, which appellant challenged on the grounds that the mandatory minimum sentences under the statute were unconstitutional because they deprived the prosecution of the discretion to request the court not to impose the mandatory sentence. The court held that, although it was the judiciary's function to impose sentences upon conviction, this function was not unconstitutionally violated by the mandatory sentencing provisions of § 3735. The court held that it was for the legislature to establish criminal sanctions, and, as one of its options, it could prescribe a mandatory minimum term of imprisonment. The court noted that the prosecutor was vested with considerable discretion in deciding who will or will not be charged and with what. By determining in the first instance whether or not to initiate a prosecution based on § 3735, the prosecutor retained his prosecutorial discretion in enforcing the criminal laws. Accordingly, there were no constitutional infirmities in § 3735.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for homicide by motor vehicle. The functions of neither the judiciary nor the prosecution were constitutionally violated by the mandatory minimum sentences of the applicable statute.

Commonwealth v. Chmiel


NO. 00522 HBG 91


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


416 Pa. Super. 235; 610 A.2d 1058; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2200


April 8, 1992, Argued  
July 22, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 16, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of July 22, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division, at No. 1340 CA 1990; 3315 CA 1990.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his judgment of sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) for involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, corruption of minors and possession of obscene or other sexual materials. Appellant argued that the mandatory sentence violated his constitutional rights and that the lower court abused its jurisdiction by disregarding the plea bargain.


OVERVIEW: Appellant had pleaded guilty on consolidated cases to involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, corruption of minors, and possession of obscene and other sexual material and performances. Appellant was sentenced with an aggregate term. Appellant then filed motions to withdraw his guilty plea and to modify sentence, which were both denied. The court found that appellant's claim that a mandatory minimum sentence was unconstitutional was meritless. The court found that the imposition of a mandatory five-year minimum prison term was not cruel or unusual, because of the nature of the crime involved here. Appellant then contended abuse of discretion by disregarding the plea bargain. The court affirmed rejecting this argument because the trial court had engaged in an exhaustive review of the case's mitigating and aggravating factors, as well as the applicable law. The trial court then imposed the minimum sentence prescribed by law and, in accordance with the previous agreement between the commonwealth and appellant, ran all the sentences concurrently. Thus the trial court followed the plea agreement and did not abuse its discretion.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the mandatory minimum sentence was not unconstitutional and the trial court did not abuse its discretion when sentencing appellant pursuant to the plea agreement.

Commonwealth v. Bowen


NO. 01761 PHILADELPHIA 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


417 Pa. Super. 340; 612 A.2d 512; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2195


January 22, 1992, Argued  
July 20, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Concurring Memorandum Ordered Not to be Published. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 6, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence April 24, 1991, In the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal, at No. 9004-2334-47.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of sentence is vacated and the case is remanded for resentencing in accordance with this opinion. Jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the judgment of sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after appellee was found guilty of three counts of robbery, conspiracy and possessing an instrument of crime. The Commonwealth contended that the sentencing court erred in failing to apply the deadly weapon enhancement provision of the sentencing guidelines.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court sentenced appellee, the Commonwealth appealed the judgment of sentence. The court reversed and remanded for resentencing. The trial court erred by failing to apply the deadly weapon enhancement of the sentencing guidelines pursuant to 24 Pa. Code § 303.4. Although the trial court convicted appellee of robbery, conspiracy and possessing an instrument of crime, it attempted to avoid this verdict by failing to enhance appellee's sentence for possession of the instrument. The record supported no other conclusion than that appellee "possessed" a deadly weapon within the meaning of Pa. Const. Stat. § 2154(b). Uncontradicted evidence established that appellee at all times either had a gun on his person or within his immediate physical control. Therefore, the trial court had no discretion to deny the application of the enhancement.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellee's sentence holding that the trial court erred in not applying the deadly weapon enhancement in determining the correct sentencing range. The enhancement was applicable because the uncontradicted evidence and the verdict established that appellee "possessed" a gun during the commission of robbery. Therefore, the case was remanded for resentencing.

In re Adoption of Steven S.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a final judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), Orphans' Court Division, terminating their parental rights to their son, who was removed from appellants' care because his home environment seriously jeopardized his health and safety.


OVERVIEW: Appellee filed a petition for the involuntary termination of appellants parental rights with regard to their son, whose home environment seriously jeopardized his health and safety. Commonwealth v. Spells


No. 02274 Philadelphia 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


417 Pa. Super. 233; 612 A.2d 458; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1989


September 12, 1991, Argued  
July 1, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted September 1, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Pa. LEXIS 179.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at Nos. 8711-1067-1070.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), sentencing her to serve a five-year mandatory minimum sentence of imprisonment for attempting to kill her estranged husband with a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of aggravated assault, simple assault, possession of an instrument of crime, and reckless endangerment of another person. Appellant's own admission indicated that she had intended to kill her estranged husband by pointing a gun at his head and pulling the trigger. The issue was whether the sentence appellant received for aggravated assault violated her constitutional rights. The court affirmed appellant's five-year mandatory sentence of imprisonment because it was not so disproportionate to the crime that it constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The seriousness of the crime with which appellant was convicted could not be questioned, and the mandatory five-year minimum sentence for offenses committed with firearms had repeatedly withstood constitutional challenge. In addition, appellant's equal protection and due process challenges were without merit.


OUTCOME: Appellant's five-year mandatory sentence of imprisonment for attempting to kill her estranged husband with a firearm was affirmed because it was proportionate to the crime and, therefore, was not cruel and unusual. Appellant's equal protection and due process challenges lacked merit.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence May 2, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal No. 8707-3498-3501

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence December 6, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal No. C.P. 87-07-3502-3505


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Eight defendants appealed from judgments of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) following their convictions of first-degree murder, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime. All defendants received the mandatory sentence of life imprisonment for the murder conviction.


OVERVIEW: Eight defendants appealed their convictions for first-degree murder, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime, which arose from their involvement in La Cosa Nostra and the killing of an individual. In pertinent part the court reversed the judgments of sentence and remanded the case for a new trial because the court found that the change in plea of one of the defendants may have resulted in the Commonwealth's intrusion into the defense's strategies and that there was a very real likelihood of prejudice to the remaining defendants. The court found that evidence of Mafia relationships was so detailed as to provide that as a requirement of becoming an initiated member, one had to participate in a killing. This evidence, along with the testimony that defendants were made members was prejudicial error for certain of the defendants and lacked probative value. The court found that the trial court abused its discretion in refusing defense counsels' request for a mistrial as a result of the prosecution referring to the defendants as "wolves" because the comments exceeded the bounds of propriety and constituted an appeal to the passions and prejudices of the jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of sentence and remanded the case for a new trial in an action in which defendants were convicted of first-degree murder, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime because the court determined that a change in plea of one defendant, evidence that defendants were "made members," and calling defendants "wolves" was prejudicial error.

Commonwealth v. Harper


NO. 02661 PHILADELPHIA 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


416 Pa. Super. 608; 611 A.2d 1211; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1598


March 19, 1992, Argued  
June 16, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of July 23, 1991, In the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Criminal, at No. 315-1990.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania), that imposed sentence upon him after a jury convicted him of one count of possession with intent to deliver cocaine and one count of simple possession of cocaine.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested after a police officer observed behavior on the part of appellant that indicated he was involved in drug trafficking. Appellant was convicted of possession with intent to deliver cocaine and possession of cocaine. Appellant challenged the conviction contending his motion to suppress should not have been denied, there was insufficient evidence to sustain the conviction, and the trial court erred in sentencing him without allowing him to present evidence as to actual amount of possession. The court found no seizure within the U.S. Const. amend IV had taken place at the time appellant discarded the clothes containing the cocaine; therefore, the cocaine was not fruits of an unlawful seizure of appellant, which was properly admitted. The court further concluded the totality of the circumstances allowed the jury to conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant possessed the cocaine with the intent to deliver it. The court further concluded appellant's sentencing argument was without merit because his denial of culpability was not properly raised at sentencing stage as a jury had already convicted him of possession.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence of the trial court finding that there was no seizure under the Fourth Amendment at the time when appellant discarded clothing containing cocaine and that the motion to suppress was properly denied. The jury was allowed to conclude drugs were possessed with the intent to deliver and denial of culpability to lessen sentencing minimum guidelines was inappropriate as jury had convicted him of possession.

Commonwealth v. Parrella


No. 641 Pittsburgh 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


416 Pa. Super. 131; 610 A.2d 1006; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1471


September 11, 1991, Argued  
June 3, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 19, 1992. Second Correction June 25, 1992. Petition for Allowance of Appeal and/or Cross-Petition Denied April 14, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Suppression Order March 01, 1991, in the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County, Criminal, No. 210 Criminal 1990



DISPOSITION: Order AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Somerset County (Pennsylvania), suppressing the tape recordings of conversations between appellee and his estranged wife in the prosecution of appellee for criminal homicide relating to the death of his wife.


OVERVIEW: The trial court ordered the suppression of tape recordings of conversations between appellee and his estranged wife in the prosecution of appellee for criminal homicide. The tapes were produced without the consent of appellee and the decedent, which occurred in an apartment on the night of her death. The tapes were made through the use of an improvised interception system consisting of an electronic baby monitor and tape recorder that was surreptitiously installed by appellee's girlfriend in the apartment where she resided with appellee. The court held that the recordation of the parties' conversation in the apartment constituted an illegal interception pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 5702 and 5703. The parties did not lose their reasonable expectations of privacy to all of their conversations merely because some portions of them were able to be heard outside the apartment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order suppressing the tape recordings of conversations between appellee and his estranged wife in the prosecution of appellee for criminal homicide, because the conversations were illegally intercepted by a surreptitiously installed electronic device.

Commonwealth v. Crowley


No. 01282 Pittsburgh 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


413 Pa. Super. 554; 605 A.2d 1256; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1148


January 21, 1992, Submitted  
April 6, 1992, Decided  
April 6, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division at No. 9016915.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to three to six years imprisonment under the mandatory sentencing statute, 18 P.S. § 7508(a)(3)(i), after his conviction for possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance. Appellant challenged his three to six year sentence and asserted that the trial court erred in basing its application of the mandatory sentencing statute, 18 P.S. § 7508(a)(3)(i), on the total mixture of cocaine and other non-controlled substances found in his possession. Appellant also argued that § 7508(a)(3)(i) was unconstitutional. The court affirmed because the trial court properly used the total weight of the mixture to determine whether a mandatory minimum sentence should be imposed under § 7508(a)(3)(i). There was a rational basis for the classification drawn under § 7508(a)(3)(i) because the legislature knew that cocaine was generally marketed in a diluted state, and an individual in possession of a larger quantity was more likely to be a dealer. Even if a statute was imprecise, a person who was given fair warning that his conduct was criminal in nature had no standing to complain that other persons who engaged in a different course of conduct may not constitutionally be prosecuted to the same extent under that statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's punishment under the mandatory sentencing statute because the trial court properly based appellant's sentence on the total weight of the mixture in his possession, including cocaine and other non-controlled substances. The statute had a rational basis because it imposed more severe punishments on those more likely to be dealers and was not unconstitutional on its face or as applied.

Shaffer v. Procter & Gamble


No. 01672 Philadelphia 1991


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


412 Pa. Super. 630; 604 A.2d 289; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 477


January 16, 1992, Argued  
March 6, 1992, Decided  
March 6, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 5, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order Entered May 17, 1991 in the Court of Common Pleas of Wyoming County, Civil, No. 90-953.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Wyoming County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee employer, rehabilitation services, and individuals' preliminary objections to appellant's causes of action for tortious conduct arising from her claim under the Worker's Compensation Act, 77 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's order sustaining appellee employer and rehabilitation services' preliminary objections to appellant employee's cause of action arising from her claim under the Worker's Compensation Act (act), 77 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1 et seq., because the act's exclusivity provision, 77 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 481, barred civil actions based on intentional torts which proceeded from work-related injuries. Appellant's claim was not viable under the Pennsylvania Human Relations Act, 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 951 et seq., because that statute covered only non-job related handicaps. The court held that it was appellant's injury and refusal to cooperate with appellee employer's unreasonable demands which resulted in the vindictive, malicious, and persecutory treatment she received, which while appalling, was job-related and, therefore, her intentional tort claims could not be raised independent of the act. Appellant's civil conspiracy claim was insufficient to satisfy the applicable standard for pleading. Finally, the fact that the act could be altered by the legislature did not affect the constitutional validity of the exclusivity provision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order sustaining appellee employer, rehabilitation services, and individuals' preliminary objections to appellant's causes of action for tortious conduct arising from her prior worker's compensation claim, because the exclusivity provision of the worker's compensation act precluded her assertion of those claims.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant priest and appellant diocese sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Somerset County, (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee sex abuse victim's motion to compel the production of documents designated by appellant diocese as part of a "secret archive."


OVERVIEW: Appellee sex abuse victim sued appellant priest for alleged pedophilic sex acts and sued appellant diocese for negligently hiring appellant priest. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County, (Pennsylvania), which imposed 36 to 72 years imprisonment following appellant's plea of guilty to seven charges of involuntary deviate sexual intercourse with children.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was a religious instructor for a well known organization and assaulted young children who attended his classes. On review, appellant argued both that his sentence was manifestly excessive and that it violated U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court affirmed the sentence and found that the trial court deviated from sentencing guidelines based upon the grave consequences of appellant's actions on his victims, the fact that appellant utilized his position as a religious instructor to gain access to his victims, and the continuing nature of appellant's crimes. Because the trial court had a presentence report, the court presumed that it considered each factor outlined by appellant. The court held that appellant's sentence was not disproportionate to sentences imposed for less reprehensible criminal behavior and that he did not prove other jurisdictions were more lenient and his constitutional challenged failed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that sentenced appellant to 36 to 72 years imprisonment. The court held that deviation from sentencing guidelines was proper based on the grave consequences of appellant's actions on his victims, the fact that appellant utilized his position as a religious instructor to gain access to his victims, and the continuing nature of appellant's crimes.

Commonwealth v. Grekis


NO. 00606 PITTSBURGH 1990


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


411 Pa. Super. 513; 601 A.2d 1284; 1992 Pa. Super. LEXIS 88


September 11, 1991, Argued  
January 14, 1992, Decided  
January 14, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence April 04, 1990, In the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal, No. 8901717.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed. Jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction and sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), for promoting prostitution, corrupting a minor, solicitation to commit prostitution, and solicitation to commit involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. Appellant alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, insufficiency of the evidence, and that the trial court erred in permitting the commonwealth to amend the information.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owned and operated a bar that was a sanctuary to numerous "prostitutes and pimps," including a 14-year-old prostitute arrested for soliciting a police officer. The original information charged appellant with criminal solicitation to commit prostitution under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5902(a), a misdemeanor of the third degree. When the commonwealth was apprised of the age of the prostitute, and the acts that appellant allegedly committed or attempted to commit with her, the information was amended to charge appellant with criminal solicitation to commit involuntary deviate sexual intercourse pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3123. The court held that the amendments were properly permitted by the trial court because the separate counts were predicated on the same three incidents and on the same facts; required the same proof, and the same conduct supported both charges; and that the law allowed changes that involved offenses with different elements. Appellant failed to properly preserve his challenges to the sufficiency of the evidence for appeal. The court held that appellant's counsel was not ineffective for failure to object to certain testimony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction and sentencing of appellant for promoting prostitution, corrupting a minor, solicitation to commit prostitution, and to commit involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. The court held that the trial court properly permitted the commonwealth to amend the information and that appellant had effective assistance of counsel. Appellant's challenges to the sufficiency of the evidence were waived.

Gross v. Johns-Manville Corp.


No. 367 Philadelphia 1991, No. 368 Philadelphia 1991, No. 369 Philadelphia 1991, No. 370 Philadelphia 1991


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


410 Pa. Super. 486; 600 A.2d 558; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3741


July 16, 1991, Argued  
December 11, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 26, 1992. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 19, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order November 5, 1990, of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division at No. 732 July Term, 1982.

Appeal from the Order December 7, 1990, of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division at No. 732 July Term, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of a jury verdict from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), awarding plaintiffs $ 170,000 in compensatory damages and $ 50,000 for loss of consortium for personal injuries suffered by plaintiff husband due to prolonged workplace asbestos exposure. Two defendants challenged the damage award as being excessive.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs challenged the amount of damages awarded by a jury for personal injuries plaintiff husband incurred from long-term asbestos exposure at his workplace. Plaintiffs appealed the trial court's denial of their motion for delay damages and sought an additur after receiving $ 170,000 in compensatory damages and $ 50,000 for loss of consortium. Two defendants cross-appealed, arguing that the damage award was excessive and seeking a remittitur. On appeal, the court held that plaintiffs had timely filed their motion for delay damages pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 238(a)-(c). The compensatory damage award, although considerably lower than plaintiffs' prayer, was a viable compromise amount and not subject to additur. Defendants were not entitled to a new trial on the issues of remittitur or delay damages because the jury award was not excessive. Because there was no evidence permitting the jury to apportion the damage award, the trial court correctly declined to instruct on the issue of comparative fault. Delay damages were appropriate on plaintiffs' loss of consortium claim. The court reversed the trial court's denial of plaintiffs' motion for delay damages but otherwise affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of plaintiffs' motion for delay damages because plaintiffs timely filed that motion within 10 days of the jury's liability finding against defendants. The court otherwise denied defendants' appeal and affirmed the jury's compensatory and loss of consortium awards, permitting delay damages but not additur or remittitur.
