Commonwealth v. Buonopane


No. 01348 Philadelphia, 1991


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


410 Pa. Super. 215; 599 A.2d 681; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3526


September 24, 1991, Argued  
November 21, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 2, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order in the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Criminal Division, No. 977 of 1990.



DISPOSITION: Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which granted defendant's pretrial motion to preclude the commonwealth from proceeding with the instant murder prosecution as a capital case on the ground that the commonwealth failed to establish the existence of an aggravating circumstance.


OVERVIEW: The commonwealth challenged the trial court's order granting defendant's motion to preclude the commonwealth from seeking the death penalty and holding that the commonwealth failed to establish the existence of an aggravating circumstance. The court reversed the trial court's order and remanded the cause because the trial court had no authority to make a pretrial determination of the presence or absence of aggravating circumstances in a trial for murder. The court ruled that the prosecution did not abuse its discretion in considering the aggravating circumstance enumerated in 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(d)(7), that defendant created a grave risk of death to others in addition to the victim. The court held that the order was reversible also on the ground that the trial court erred in compelling a deputy district attorney to testify, without any offer of proof or legal basis from the defense, regarding procedures used by the district attorney in evaluating murder cases for presentation as capital or noncapital offenses, and in making a baseless finding concerning a lack of aggravating circumstances where no evidence was presented on that issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order precluding the commonwealth from proceeding with the murder prosecution as a capital case and remanded the case. The court held that the trial court was not authorized to make a pretrial determination as to the capital or noncapital nature of a murder prosecution.

Commonwealth v. John

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order entered December 19, 1990, Court of Common Pleas, Westmoreland County, Criminal Division at No. 34 C 1990.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered December 19, 1990, Court of Common Pleas, Westmoreland County, Criminal Division at No. 34 C 1990.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him following convictions for arson, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3301(a)(1)(i), risking a catastrophe, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3302(b), and criminal mischief, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3304(a)(1). The commonwealth sought permission to appeal under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9781(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of arson, risking a catastrophe, and criminal mischief for setting fire to a building where his wife was playing bingo. The commonwealth filed a challenge to the discretionary aspect of the sentence. The court affirmed and denied the commonwealth's appeal under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9781(b), which required a substantial question. The court found that the state merely requested the court to substitute its judgment for that of the sentencing court. OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court sentencing defendant for arson, risking a catastrophe, and criminal mischief was affirmed because the trial court did not err in admitting testimony regarding defendant's conduct after the crime, the testimony of his spouse fell within the exception to the privilege, counsel was not ineffective, and there was sufficient evidence. The court denied the commonwealth's appeal because there was no substantial question.

Superior Court of Pennsylvania

Commonwealth v. Bagley


No. 1328 Philadelphia, 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


408 Pa. Super. 188; 596 A.2d 811; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2177


November 9, 1990, Argued  
July 30, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Reargument Denied September 27, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 22, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Delaware County, No. 8188 of 1989.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed in part, affirmed in part and remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing Opinion. Jurisdiction is not retained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which suppressed evidence seized pursuant to warrants issued relative to the investigation of the suspicious death of appellee's wife. The state asserted that suppression of the evidence was improper and that it was unable to proceed without the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was suspected of killing his wife after a nurse alerted authorities of suspicious circumstances. As a result of the tip, police obtained search warrants which resulted in the culmination of evidence against appellee, the husband. The lower court suppressed all evidence and the state sought review. On appeal, the court examined the warrants and the circumstances. The court observed that one of the search warrants was invalid because it failed to identify any crime which had been committed and it did not particularly describe the property for which the warrant had been issued. However, the court held that evidence seized from appellee's home was lawfully obtained by police pursuant to appellee's voluntary consent. The court found that evidence obtained via inspection of an electrical system, a recreation of appellee's version of his wife's death, and the taking of photos and video of the home were properly authorized by search warrants. The court did agree and affirm the suppression of certain documentary evidence which consisted of personal effects that did not constitute evidence of the commission of a crime. The decision was otherwise reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's suppression of documentary evidence consisting of personal effects which did not constitute evidence of the commission of a crime. However, the decision to suppress the other evidence was reversed and remanded because, although the warrant failed to identify any crime which had been committed and it did not particularly describe the property, appellee gave voluntary consent to the search and seizure.

Domestic Relations Section for Diehl v. Mulhern


No. 684 Pittsburgh, 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


406 Pa. Super. 422; 594 A.2d 692; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2017


April 10, 1991, Argued  
July 24, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Family Division, of Cambria County, No. DR 285-79.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant alleged father challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County (Pennsylvania), that directed appellant to pay appellee mother child support for the child at issue, who was born out of wedlock. Appellant contended that such order was erroneous, where there had never been a determination made that he was the father of the child, either by paternity order or acknowledgement.


OVERVIEW: In this proceeding for the support of a child born out of wedlock to appellee mother, the trial court entered an order directing appellant alleged father to pay support. The judgment was reversed because the record was clear that there had been neither an adjudication of paternity, nor an acknowledgement by appellant that he was the father of the child. The court held that the obligation of a parent to support his or her child was an unwavering one, and one parent was not permitted to contract away the right of his or her child to seek support from the other parent. Appellant was correct, however, that before he could be compelled to contribute to the support of the child born out of wedlock, there should have been a determination that he was the father of the child. Appellant was also correct that there had been no such determination in this case. The lump sum settlement could not be deemed a legally binding acknowledgement of paternity. Appellant had consistently denied that he was the father of the child born to appellee, and the record made at the time of the lump sum settlement contained no admission of paternity.


OUTCOME: The order directing appellant alleged father to pay child support to appellee mother was reversed because there had neither been an adjudication of paternity, nor an acknowledgement by appellant, that he was the father of the child, who was born out of wedlock. Accordingly, the court held that ordering payments of child support was erroneous.

Commonwealth v. Green

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), that imposed a mandatory minimum sentence pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508, after her conviction of controlled substances offenses, contending that under the circumstances of her age and health the sentence constituted cruel and unusual punishment in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possession of a controlled substance, possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver, and possession of drug paraphernalia, and was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of not less than two and not more than four years pursuant to the mandatory minimum sentencing provisions of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508. Commonwealth v. Murphy


No. 03027 Philadelphia, 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


405 Pa. Super. 452; 592 A.2d 750; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1630


April 15, 1991, Submitted  
June 14, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County, Criminal Division, at No. 2748-89, 2748.1-89.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a sentence from the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which imposed concurrent terms for possession of controlled substances, possession of controlled substances with the intent to deliver, and conspiracy. Because the offense of possession with intent to deliver was committed within 1,000 feet of a school, the court added 12 months to the sentence pursuant to 204 Pa. Cons. Ann. § 303.9(c)(2).


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted appellant of possession of controlled substances, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 780-113(a)(16); possession of controlled substances with the intent to deliver, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 780-113(a)(30); and conspiracy. Appellant received three concurrent sentences. The trial court found that it was compelled, pursuant to the enhancement provision of 204 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 303.9(c)(2), to add 12 months to the sentence for possession with intent to deliver because the offense was committed within 1,000 feet of a school. On appeal, the court affirmed in part, vacated in part, and remanded for resentencing. The court held that the crimes of possession and possession with intent to deliver should have been merged for sentencing purposes. Concluding that the trial court had imposed an illegal sentence, the court vacated the sentence for possession. The court found that the trial court erred in treating the enhancement provision as mandatory. Accordingly, the court also vacated the sentence for possession with intent to deliver. The court rejected appellant's argument that tit. 204, § 303.9(c)(2) required proof of intent to be within 1,000 feet of a school.


OUTCOME: Finding that the trial court erred in failing to merge the offenses of possession of controlled substances and possession of controlled substances with the intent to deliver, and in treating as mandatory the school-zone enhancement provision, the court vacated the sentences for possession and possession with intent to deliver, and remanded for resentencing. The court affirmed the sentence imposed for conspiracy.

Commonwealth v. Dickerson


No. 01673 PHL 89


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


404 Pa. Super. 249; 590 A.2d 766; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1254


March 5, 1991, Argued  
May 8, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted October 3, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of May 19, 1989 in the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Criminal Division at No. TR. No. 9159-86.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), Criminal Division that sentenced him for rape as a recidivist under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714 although he had not been convicted of the first rape when he committed the second one. Appellant claimed that his counsel was ineffective for not objecting to the enhancement of his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant raped two different women within one hour of each other. As a result, appellant was convicted of the first rape and pled guilty to the second rape. The trial court sentenced appellant as a recidivist for the second rape under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9714 and used the first rape in calculating appellant's sentence under the guidelines. On appeal, the court held that because appellant had not been convicted of the first rape at the time he committed his second rape, the first rape could not be used as an enhancer under § 9714. However, the trial court could consider the prior rape in fashioning the sentence. Because the trial court indicated that it used the sentencing guidelines as guidance in determining appellant's sentence, the use of the first rape to calculate appellant's sentence prejudiced him. However, because there was no hearing to determine why appellant's counsel failed to object to the sentence, the court vacated the sentence and remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing on appellant's ineffective assistance of counsel claim, for a new sentencing hearing if counsel was ineffective, or reimposition of the sentence if counsel was effective.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's sentence for rape and remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing to determine if appellant's counsel was effective. The court held that because appellant was not convicted of his first rape before he committed the second one, the first rape could not enhance his sentence. If appellant's counsel was found to be ineffective for not objecting to the enhancement, appellant was entitled to a new sentencing hearing.

Commonwealth v. Logan


No. 225 Pittsburgh 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


404 Pa. Super. 100; 590 A.2d 300; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 871


December 5, 1990, Argued  
April 8, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised April 11, 1991. As Corrected May 3, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied August 26, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division at No. CC 8810468.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence vacated. Case remanded for imposition of sentence in accordance with 18 Pa.C.S.A. § 7508(a)(3)(iii).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of possession with intent to deliver cocaine, possession of cocaine, and possession of drug paraphernalia, and sentenced appellee to the mandatory minimum sentence of four years in prison.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was convicted of possession with intent to deliver cocaine, possession of cocaine, and possession of drug paraphernalia. Appellee was then sentenced to the mandatory minimum sentence of four years in prison. Thereafter, appellee filed a motion to modify sentence, which the trial court granted, and sentenced appellee to one to three years in prison. Appellant sought the court's review. The court held that under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7508(a)(3)(iii), which contained the mandatory sentence for drug trafficking, a person who was convicted of drug offenses, where the drug was cocaine, had to be sentenced to a mandatory minimum term of imprisonment of four years in prison. Further, there was no authority in any court to impose on an offender to which § 7508 applied a lesser sentence that the sentence provided under § 7508(a)(3)(iii). Here, the facts clearly revealed that appellee possessed the intent to deliver in excess of 100 grams of cocaine. Therefore, it was error for the trial court, for whatever reason, to impose a sentence less than that mandated by § 7508(a)(3)(iii). Judgment of sentence vacated and case remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: Order imposing sentence upon appellee was reversed and the case remanded for resentencing where the trial court erred in sentencing appellee to a sentence that was less than the statutory mandatory sentence following appellee's drug trafficking conviction.

Commonwealth v. Dotzman


Nos. 1869, 1870, 1871 and 1872 Philadelphia 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


403 Pa. Super. 325; 588 A.2d 1312; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 868


January 31, 1991, Argued  
April 5, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 5, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals From Judgment of Sentence May 30, 1990 In Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Philadelphia County, Nos. 8903-1629-1631, 8905-112-113, 8903-1622-1623-1625, 8905-2406, 8904-3052-3053.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence vacated and case remanded for resentencing. See Commonwealth v. Drumgoole, supra.

Panel jurisdiction is relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that declined to apply the deadly weapon enhancement, 204 Pa. Code § 303.4, to the guideline sentence range for robbery in the first-degree, where the offenses were committed at knifepoint. The commonwealth urged that § 303.4 did not implicate double jeopardy, and was not discretionary.


OVERVIEW: Appellee committed three robberies, in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3701, using a knife. As the trial court concluded that the use of a knife elevated the robberies to first-degree felonies carrying enhanced sentences, it declined to apply the guideline enhancement for use of a deadly weapon, 204 Pa. Code § 303.4, on the theory that this constituted two punishments for the same offense and violated the double jeopardy provisions of U.S. Const. amend. V and Pa. Const. art. I, § 10. In vacating the sentence imposed and remanding for resentencing, the court pointed out several errors in the trial court's reasoning. The court first noted jurisdiction, holding that that the appeal complied with Pa. R. App. P. 2119(f) and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9781(b). On the merits, the court explained that robbery could be a first-degree felony without the use of a deadly weapon. Moreover, where a deadly weapon was used, the triggering of the § 303.4 enhancement provision was not a double punishment but only a requirement that the guideline sentence range be increased. Finally, consideration of § 303.4 was mandatory, not discretionary, although discretion existed to fix the ultimate sentence.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellee's judgment of sentence and remanded for resentencing. The trial court erred in declining to consider the deadly weapon enhancement for application to the guideline sentence range, as consideration of the enhancement was mandatory. Application of the enhancement was not double jeopardy, as it merely increased the guideline sentence range where a deadly weapon had been used.

Commonwealth v. McCauley


No. 528 Harrisburg, 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


403 Pa. Super. 262; 588 A.2d 941; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 733


January 22, 1991, Argued  
March 28, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 4, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied February 14, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Juniata County, Criminal Division, No. 141 of 1988.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged decision from Court of Common Pleas of Juniata County (Pennsylvania) convicting appellant of second degree murder pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2502(b). Appellant argued it was error to admit testimony of a forensic criminologist, that investigative delay deprived him of due process rights, and the prosecution impermissibly impeached his own witness.


OVERVIEW: On appeal from conviction of second degree murder pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons Stat. § 2502(b), the court affirmed, concluding that the trial court did not err in the conviction and life sentence. Appellant asserted that was error to admit the testimony of a forensic criminologist. The court concluded that microscopic hair comparison evidence satisfied the Frye standard. The criminologist's testimony was relevant insofar as it was more probative than prejudicial. It did not mislead, prejudice or confuse the jury but gave a demonstrable accepted form of tying appellant to the crime. The court further held that prosecuting a defendant after investigative delay did not deprive him of due process even if the defense was somewhat prejudiced by the lapse of time. Also, counsel could permissibly impeach his own witness on a plea of surprise when the witness gave testimony which was contradictory to statements which the witness had made earlier and were harmful to the party calling the witness while beneficial to the opposing side. In addition, the courts were liberal in allowing a party to cross-examine his own witness when it was believed that the interests of truth and justice required it.


OUTCOME: The conviction of second degree murder and imposition of life sentence were affirmed. The court concluded that the prosecution could permissibly impeach his or her own witness on a plea of surprise when the witness gave testimony which was contradictory to statements which the witness had made earlier. In addition, microscopic hair comparison evidence was a demonstrably accepted form of tying appellant to the crime.

 

371 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Corradino


No. 00827 Philadelphia 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


403 Pa. Super. 251; 588 A.2d 936; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 666


December 11, 1990, Argued  
March 26, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of February 16, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County, Criminal Division, at No. 719 of 1986.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania), where he had been convicted for violating the Uniform Firearms Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5115, loaning a firearm to another. The trial court had admitted computer printouts over appellant's objection, and had denied his demurrer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant had been convicted for violating the Uniform Firearms Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6104-6124, lending a firearm to another. Appellant challenged his sentence, contending that the trial court erred in admitting into evidence computer printouts and in denying his demurrer. The court affirmed. The printouts were not hearsay. Their admissibility in evidence was governed by the hearsay exception for regularly kept records. Testimony provided a sufficient indication of reliability of the printouts to warrant their admission. Proof that a person loaned a firearm was sufficient to sustain a conviction under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6115. Appellant's admission that he had loaned his firearm was sufficient to support a finding of guilt. The trial court, therefore, did not err in denying appellant's demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence because the computer printouts he alleged had been erroneously admitted as hearsay had been properly admitted under the business records exception to the hearsay rule. Appellant's demurrer had been properly denied, because he had admitted to the offense of loaning a firearm to another.

 

372 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Gonzalez


No. 405 Philadelphia 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


403 Pa. Super. 157; 588 A.2d 528; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 663


January 9, 1991, Argued  
March 22, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal at No. 516 April Term, 1988.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment rendered by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that convicted him of cruelty to animals in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5511(h.1).


OVERVIEW: During a search of appellant's residence, police discovered roosters in cages that were groomed for cock fighting. Appellant was convicted of cruelty to animals in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5511. On appeal, appellant claimed that the delegation of police power to animal welfare agents under § 5511 to initiate felony proceedings, to make arrests, and to seize and destroy forfeited property was unconstitutional. The court noted that the involvement of the animal welfare agents was peripheral to an undisputedly valid search warrant. The court therefore held that appellant was without standing to complain about § 5511's investigation and arrest provisions because he failed to show that he was injured. Appellant also argued that § 5511 was preempted by a federal statute, 7 U.S.C.S. § 2156. The court disagreed, holding that Congress did not intend to pre-empt completely the field of animal fighting legislation. Finally, appellant asserted that § 5511 was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The court concluded that appellant lacked standing to challenge the statute's overbreadth because his conduct clearly fell within its prohibitions. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence because it held that appellant was without standing to challenge the constitutionality of a state animal cruelty statute and that the statute was not preempted by federal legislation.

In re Estate of Greenwood


No. 653 Pittsburgh 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


402 Pa. Super. 536; 587 A.2d 749; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 633


December 4, 1990, Argued  
March 11, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition For Allowance of Appeal Denied October 25, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Greene County, Civil Division at No. O.C. No. 7 of 1990.
Greenwood Estate, 5 Pa. D. & C.4th 665, 1990 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 369 (1990)



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administratrix sought review of order directing estate deceased's blood samples be made available to the domestic relations division under Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2107, in the Court of Common Pleas of Green County (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Appellant administratrix sought review of orphan's court order directing estate deceased's blood sample be made available for purposes of appellee possible illegitimate daughter's establishing an inheritance right based upon an allegation that the deceased was her father. Appellant contended the statute of limitations contained in 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 4343(b) barred appellee alleged daughter from such samples because of a failure to take any action to establish paternity within 18 years of her birthdate. The court rejected the argument and affirmed the order. The court explained the statute of limitations for establishing paternity for purposes of child support was inapplicable to the present case where paternity would be the basis for intestate succession. The court determined the right to inherit was reserved exclusively to the Probate, Estates, and Fiduciaries Code, 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 2101 et seq. (Supp. 1990). The court further noted the standard for proving a right to intestate succession was clear and convincing evidence and the tests might conclusively eliminate the deceased as appellee's father.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed order directing appellant administratrix to make blood samples available for purposes of appellee alleged illegitimate daughter's attempt to establish her right to intestate succession because statute of limitations period for establishing paternity for child support was inapplicable to the establishment of rights to inherit which was governed by the estates and probate code.

Commonwealth v. Purcell

Superior Court of Pennsylvania


1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 423

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimed ineffective assistance of counsel in his appeal from the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), after he was tried by jury, found guilty of indecent assault and corruption of a minor, and was sentenced.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his convictions of indecent assault and corruption of a minor and his sentences on grounds that he had ineffective assistance of counsel at trial. The court's review of the record disclosed that there was arguable merit in appellant's contention that trial counsel rendered constitutionally ineffective assistance at his jury trial and that appellant was prejudiced thereby. The court ordered that the judgment of sentence be vacated, at least for the time being, and the case be remanded for an evidentiary hearing on appellant's claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. The court instructed that if counsel were determined to have acted reasonably, or if appellant was not prejudiced by counsel's conduct, sentence could be reimposed. In the meantime, jurisdiction was not retained by the court.


OUTCOME: The court waived the judgment of sentence, vacated it for the time being, and remanded for an evidentiary hearing because it found arguable merit in appellant's claim of ineffective assistance of counsel at his jury trial that resulted in convictions of indecent assault and corruption of a minor.

Commonwealth v. Leib


No. 166 Harrisburg 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


403 Pa. Super. 223; 588 A.2d 922; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 383


October 3, 1990, Argued  
February 14, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied April 11, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of February 12, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Criminal Division, at No. 1609 CA 1987.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of driving under the influence conviction under Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731 (a) in favor of appellee state in the Court of Common Pleas of York County Criminal Division (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his driving under influence conviction under Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a), in favor of appellee state, based on numerous urged errors. The court rejected the argument that the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict when the jury was free to discredit appellant's claim that he consumed the beer that raised his blood alcohol level while waiting for aid for his broken down car. The court noted the record showed several alcohol cans in the car, appellant was found slumped over the wheel, the keys were in the ignition, and he failed a blood alcohol test forty-five minutes after he was found. The court next rejected the argument that appellee state failed to bring him to trial within speedy trial limits. The court explained that where the state despite due diligence could not bring appellant to trial because of complex pre-trial motions filed by appellant, its failure to do so did not entitle appellant to a discharge. The court noted although the record revealed trial was held over for two years, pending pre-trial defense trial motions prevented the case from being tried on time. The court found blood tests were properly admitted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction, in favor of appellee state, because sufficient evidence was presented to support verdict of driving under the influence when appellant was operating the vehicle although he was not driving it, blood alcohol tests were properly admitted, and appellee's failure to bring him to trial within speedy trial limits was excused when based upon complex defense pre-trial motions.

Jenkins v. Hospital of the Medical College of Pa.


No. 3594 Philadelphia 1988


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


401 Pa. Super. 604; 585 A.2d 1091; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 204


May 31, 1990, Argued  
January 31, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted March 26, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order Entered December 6, 1988, in the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Philadelphia County, No. 5561 February 1986.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that granted the motion of appellees, hospital and doctors, to strike certain paragraphs from appellant's complaint for stating a wrongful birth claim, which was barred by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8305(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother brought a negligence action against appellees, hospital and doctors, asserting that if appropriate diagnostic procedures had been performed during her pregnancy, enabling her to learn that her unborn child was afflicted with Down's syndrome, she would have terminated the pregnancy and chosen not to undergo a tubal ligation. After the trial court struck portions of appellant's complaint for stating a wrongful birth claim, which was prohibited by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8305(a), appellant challenged. In reversing, the court held that the application of § 8305(a) to appellant's claim was unconstitutional because appellant's cause of action accrued on March 8, 1984, more than four years before the promulgation and effective date of § 8305(a). The court stated that because a cause of action in tort accrued on the date of the accident or injury, appellant's cause of action accrued on March 8, 1984, the date on which the allegedly wrongful birth of her son occurred. The court noted that § 8305(a) could not have been applied retroactively to extinguish a right of action that had already accrued to appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's order that barred appellant mother's cause of action for wrongful birth pursuant to statutory law because the trial court's application of such law was unconstitutionally retrospective in that appellant's cause of action for wrongful birth accrued on the date of her son's birth, four years prior to the statute's effective date.

Commonwealth v. Bleigh


Nos. 00100 Harrisburg 1990, 00101 Harrisburg 1990, 00102 Harrisburg 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


402 Pa. Super. 169; 586 A.2d 450; 1991 Pa. Super. LEXIS 14


December 13, 1990, Argued  
January 9, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Judgment of Sentence Entered January 11, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Nos. 1690, 1710 and 1717 Criminal 1988.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, two female dancers and an adult bookstore owner, sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania) that convicted them of various prostitution charges and drug possession offenses.


OVERVIEW: Undercover police officers paid two female dancers at an adult bookstore money to watch them masturbate. A search warrant was issued for the bookstore. The marked bills given to the dancers were recovered, along with drug paraphernalia found in one dancer's purse and the bookstore owner's briefcase. The first dancer was convicted of prostitution, the second dancer of prostitution and drug possession, and the owner of promoting prostitution and drug possession. The court reversed all the convictions relating to prostitution. The court held that self-masturbation for hire without any physical contact between performer and viewer was not the type of conduct intended to come within the purview of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5902(a). However, the court affirmed the drug convictions. The court held that the search warrant was valid and based on personal observations of the police. The court held that the purse and briefcase were properly subject to search under the warrant. The court held that the drugs were in plain view in the purse and briefcase.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions of appellants that related to prostitution because self-masturbation for hire without any physical contact between performer and viewer did not amount to prostitution. However, the court affirmed the drug convictions of two appellants because the search warrant yielding the drug evidence was valid.

Craig v. Magee Memorial Rehabilitation Center


No. 00487 Philadelphia 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


400 Pa. Super. 126; 582 A.2d 1376; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3387


September 10, 1990, Argued  
December 7, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order dated January 26, 1990 Docketed January 29, 1990 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County Civil No. 4626 April 1976.



DISPOSITION: We, therefore, affirm the order of January 26, 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital sought review of judgment imposing delay damages in favor of appellees pursuant to state Supreme Ct. R. 238, in a medical malpractice suit in the Philadelphia County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Appellant hospital sought review of judgment imposing delay damages under state Supreme Ct. R. 238, in favor of appellees, in a malpractice suit based on several challenges to the validity of the rule. The court affirmed the imposition of such damages. The court rejected the argument that the rule deprived a defendant of property without due process of law. The court explained a defendant was not subject to such damages unless the ultimate jury award ultimately exceeded his written settlement offer by more than 125 percent. The court reasoned the fact of whether a plaintiff refused to make a reasonable demand was irrelevant to the issue of whether a defendant's offer was adequate. The court next rejected the contention that the rule was in excess of the state supreme court's authority because it affected substantive rights. The court noted the real purpose of the rule was a procedural device to encourage prompt settlement and to unclutter the court system , yet it did nonetheless have a substantive effect on parties' rights. The court explained such incidental effect upon substantive rights could not alone render the rule unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed judgment imposing delay damages upon appellant hospital in appellees' favor because state court rule imposing such damages where an ultimate verdict exceeded a defendant's settlement offer by more than 125 percent was a valid procedural device to encourage prompt settlements and unclutter courts and did not deny property without due process or exceed court's authority to promulgate due to incidental effect on substantive rights.

Ballestrino v. Ballestrino


No. 02057 PHL 89


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


400 Pa. Super. 237; 583 A.2d 474; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3383


May 2, 1990, Submitted  
December 6, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of April 22, 1988, Docketed June 20, 1989 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Family Division, at No. 7862 March 1982.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the order of April 22, 1988 quashed. Order of June 20, 1989 affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife challenged the orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that held that a prior support order incorporated in the parties' divorce decree could be modified and reduced appellee husband's alimony obligation in response to appellee's petition to remit and reduce alimony.


OVERVIEW: Appellee husband filed a petition to remit and reduce alimony paid to appellant wife. In response, the trial court entered two orders. The first order held that a prior support order incorporated in the parties' divorce decree could be modified. The second order reduced appellee's monthly support obligation. The court affirmed the second order and held that the support agreement merged into the divorce decree and was subject to modification. The court was also reluctant to hold that the agreement was not modifiable where the trial court had previously used its vast powers of contempt and attachment to enforce the support order at appellant's request and cited the principle of judicial estoppel as support for denying appellant's claim.


OUTCOME: The court quashed appellant wife's appeal of the first order that held that a prior support order incorporated in their divorce decree could be modified because it was untimely and not final. The court affirmed the second order and held that the support agreement merged into the divorce decree and was subject to modification. The agreement was not a detailed contract, and appellant had previously enforced the agreement under the Divorce Code.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Columbia County (Pennsylvania) which dismissed appellant's fourth petition seeking post-conviction relief based on ineffective assistance of counsel on his third petition seeking post-conviction relief.

Commonwealth v. Strunk


No. 00349 Philadelphia 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


400 Pa. Super. 25; 582 A.2d 1326; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3367


June 13, 1990, Argued  
December 3, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 6, 1991. Appeal Denied September 10, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of January 16, 1990, in the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Criminal Division, at No. 556-1989.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County's (Pennsylvania) judgment of sentence, which imposed a 90-day driver's license suspension based upon underaged appellant's conviction for possession of an alcoholic beverage. Appellant contended that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6310.4 (1990) violated his substantive due process rights and the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who was underaged, was convicted of possession of an alcoholic beverage under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308 (1983) and the trial court suspended his driver's license for 90-days pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6310.4 (1990). Appellant challenged the constitutionality of § 6310.4 on both substantive due process and cruel and unusual punishment grounds. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence and stated that the imposition of § 6310.4 had not violated appellant's constitutional rights. The court stated that § 6310.4 was rationally related to the state's goals of deterrence and punishment of underaged drinking and that the imposition of § 6310.4 was a more effective deterrent than a monetary fine. Accordingly, the court found that appellant's substantive due process rights were not violated. The court also concluded that the penalty imposed pursuant to § 6310.4 was not cruel and unusual punishment because the penalty was not grossly disproportionate to the underlying offense and the general public's view that underaged possession and consumption of alcohol were serious offenses that required punishment capable of deterring future violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which imposed a 90-day suspension of underaged appellant's driver's license, based upon appellant's conviction for possession of an alcoholic beverage. The court stated that appellant's constitutional rights to substantive due process were not violated by statute's penalty and that the statute's penalty was not cruel and unusual punishment.

Commonwealth v. Munchinski


No. 01373 Pittsburgh, 1987


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


401 Pa. Super. 300; 585 A.2d 471; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3356


June 19, 1990, Argued  
November 30, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied February 11, 1991. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 13, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County, Criminal Division, No. 755 of 1982, 755 1/3 of 1982.
Com. v. Munchinski, 405 Pa. Super. 630, 581 A.2d 975, 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2512 (1990)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of sentnece of the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County (Pennsylvania) that was entered upon his convictions of two counts of first-degree murder and two counts of second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and a co-defendant were charged with criminal homicide and criminal conspiracy for the deaths of two persons. The court of common pleas declared a mistrial when the jury was unable to reach a verdict. On appeal, appellant alleged error in the court of common pleas' refusal to quash the informations. The court denied the motion to quash, severed the cases, and remanded the case for trial. On remand, the conspiracy charge against appellant was dropped. Appellant was then convicted by the jury of both first and second-degree murder as to each victim. Appellant filed a motion for a new trial. The court affirmed, holding that a mistrial was not warranted. The court held that although appellant was prosecuted for conspiracy charges beyond the statute of limitations in the first trial, there was no prejudice because the charges were dropped in the second trial. There was also no error in the court of common pleas' refusal to grant immunity to appellant's co-defendant in return for testimony. Appellant's conviction for both first and second-degree murder was proper because appellant was charged with each offense, and the jury was free to find guilt on both charges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence entered upon appellant's convictions of first and second-degree murder because there was no error in the denial of immunity to appellant's co-defendant, a mistrial was not warranted, and appellant's prosecution for conspiracy beyond the statute of limitations did not result in prejudice.

Tony Savatt, Inc. v. Latrobe Brewing Co.


No. 1771 Pittsburgh, 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


400 Pa. Super. 296; 583 A.2d 796; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3221


August 22, 1990, Argued  
November 2, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 14, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Decree of November 3, 1989, docketed November 6, 1989, Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County, Civil Division at No. G.D. 88-13932.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a brewing company, sought review of an injunction issued by the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), that prevented appellant from terminating a distribution contract with appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a brewing company, and appellee, a distributor, had an ongoing exclusive distribution agreement concerning appellant's beer. The legislature amended the Liquor Code, 47 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 4-431(d)(1), to require the termination of such a distribution agreement to be with good cause. The trial court entered an injunction preventing appellant from terminating appellee as their distributor. The court affirmed. The court held that the Liquor Code was not a generic economic regulation of the relationship between contracting parties, but, rather, was an exercise of police power for the protection of the public welfare based upon the constitutional delegation to the states of plenary power to control the sale and use of alcoholic beverages. The court held said statute to be constitutional. The court held that the statute was both reasonable and rational under the commonwealth's police power. The court also held that the statute was not retroactively applied to appellant because appellant did not have a vested right to sell alcohol and the evidence indicated that appellant intended appellee to be the primary distributor under contract.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the injunction preventing appellant from terminating a distribution contract with appellee because the statute requiring liquor distribution contracts to be terminated only for good cause was constitutional and no good cause existed for the termination of appellee's contract.

Commonwealth v. Wolfe


No. 163 Philadelphia 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


398 Pa. Super. 94; 580 A.2d 857; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2865


April 23, 1990, Submitted  
September 27, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Post Conviction Relief Act, December 11, 1989 in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal, No. 2631, 2632-86.



DISPOSITION: Order Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought relief from an order of the Common Pleas Court of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition for post-conviction relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9541 et seq. Convicted of terroristic threats and sexual offenses perpetrated on his step-daughter, appellant was sentenced to prison. His direct appeal of the sentencing order had resulted in the order's affirmance.


OVERVIEW: On appeal of the trial court's denial of appellant's petition for post-conviction relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9541 et seq., the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. Focusing solely on sentencing, appellant contended that the trial court abused its discretion and that his counsel was ineffective for failing to put forth mitigating factors for the trial court to consider. The court held that appellant's ineffective assistance of counsel claims did not relate to ineffectiveness which so undermined the truth determining process such that no reliable adjudication of guilt or innocence could have taken place, as required under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9543(a)(2)(ii). The court therefore determined that appellant's claims were not cognizable under the PCRA and that the trial court's dismissal of appellant's petition seeking collateral relief had been proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant's petition for post-conviction relief based on ineffective assistance of appellate counsel with regard to sentencing, because appellant was not able to set forth a cognizable claim pursuant to which relief could be granted under the Post Conviction Relief Act.

 

388 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Nieves


No. 1657 Philadelphia 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


399 Pa. Super. 277; 582 A.2d 341; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2863


January 18, 1990, Argued  
September 26, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied November 27, 1990. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 17, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence May 16, 1989 in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County Criminal No. 2017 - 1987.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced appellant after convicting him for the knife point sexual assault of a 12 year old girl. Appellant further challenged the denial of his motion to modify sentence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to a term of eight to 20 years imprisonment as a result of several convictions arising from his knife point sexual assault of a 12 year old girl. As a result of the assault, the child contracted gonorrhea. On appeal, appellant's allegations of error were deemed to be without merit and the judgment below was affirmed. Among other things, the court noted that statutorily privileged confidential medical records must have been disclosed to the Commonwealth in sexual abuse cases where the presence or absence of venereal disease was relevant to an issue at trial. The court also found that standard gonorrhea tests fell within the exception to the rule that medical opinions were not admissible as business records, but instead required live testimony by a qualified medical expert. With respect to the Rape Shield Law, the court found that appellant made no proffer whatsoever of an alternate explanation for victim's infection. Moreover, the court found that even if he had do so, it would not have precluded appellant as the actual source of the infection. The probative value of such evidence was minimal and the potential prejudice was high.


OUTCOME: The judgment sentencing appellant for the sexual assault of a 12 year old girl was affirmed, because there was no merit to appellant's issues regarding privacy claims pertaining to medical records subpoenaed in criminal trials, the admissibility of gonorrhea results under the business records exception to the hearsay rule, and the proper application of the Rape Shield statute.

 

389 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Gowan


Nos. 01853 Pittsburgh, 1989, 01852 Pittsburgh, 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


399 Pa. Super. 477; 582 A.2d 879; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2890


June 26, 1990, Argued  
September 25, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied December 4, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Cambia County, Criminal Division, Nos. 992-87, 0996-87.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence vacated and appellants discharged. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas, Cambia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted both of disorderly conduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were preachers and were arrested for disorderly conduct after they loudly preached in a public park. They were convicted and sought review. Appellants argued that the arrests violated their first amendment right to exercise free speech. The court vacated the appellants' convictions. It found that the commonwealth failed to establish by sufficient evidence that appellants made an unreasonable noise as required by 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5503(a)(2). Furthermore, because free speech was implicated, the court was required to balance the fundamental right to free speech against the annoyance or inconvenience to others. The court found that when a protected first amendment right to free speech was implicated, it was necessary that the actor intend to breach the public peace by making unreasonable noise. Because the appellants did not make unreasonable noise, there was no violation.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellants' convictions for disorderly conduct because the commonwealth failed to establish by sufficient evidence that appellants' preaching to a passerby in a public park constituted unreasonable noise.

In re Contempt Citation of O'Brien


No. 64 Harrisburg 1989


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4162



September 20, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appeal from the Order entered January 24, 1989, in the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal Division at Misc. No. 89-16.

Original Opinion of September 20, 1990, Reported at 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3081 



DISPOSITION: Vacated and discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant counsel challenged a judgment of the Criminal Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania), which summarily convicted him for contempt of court for asking a rape victim a line of questioning about her previous sexual behavior contrary to the court's order that was issued, pursuant to the Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the counsel of record in a rape charge against an accused. On the second day of trial, the trial court ordered the defense to refrain from asking questions regarding the victim's prior sexual experiences, pursuant to the Rape Shield Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104. Contending that his only intent was to determine the victim's tendency to fabricate her sexual involvement with others, appellant maintained that the statute violated the accused's constitutional right to confrontation of witnesses insofar as the statute prohibited admission of evidence demonstrating bias, prejudice, or which attacks the witness's credibility. Appellant was summarily convicted of contempt of court and was fined for the three questions to the victim. On appeal, the court held that appellant was zealously defending his client, and his line of inquiry was not intended to obstruct the judicial proceedings or imperiled fairness of the proceeding or the court's authority. It vacated the contempt order, and discharged appellant.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting appellant counsel for contempt of court due to his questioning of a rape victim was vacated, because appellant's behavior was not intended to obstruct the judicial proceedings or challenge the authority of the court, but rather to zealously defend his client. He was discharged.

Commonwealth v. Welshans


No. 00585 HBG 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


397 Pa. Super. 439; 580 A.2d 379; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2772


May 21, 1990, Submitted  
September 19, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of September 19, 1989 in the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Criminal Division, at No. 88-10, 950.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County (Pennsylvania) for driving under the influence of alcohol and homicide by vehicle while driving under the influence of alcohol. Appellant argued that the trial court erred when it refused to suppress results of a blood alcohol test, that trial counsel was ineffective, and that the sentence imposed was unlawful.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol and homicide by vehicle while driving under the influence of alcohol. The court vacated appellant's sentence for driving under the influence, under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731(a), because it was a lesser included offense of homicide by vehicle while driving while under the influence, under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3735(a). The court held that the trial court did not err when it refused to suppress the blood alcohol test because there was probable cause to believe that appellant was driving while under the influence and to take the sample. Appellant smelled of alcohol and was involved in a single vehicle accident when the weather was clear and the road conditions were good. The court held that appellant's counsel was not ineffective. The court held that it was not ineffective to not raise objections based on the chain of custody of the blood alcohol test and on the blood typing tests because those arguments were meritless. The argument that defense counsel should have objected to a pathologist's expert testimony was also meritless.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellant's sentence for driving under the influence and affirmed the sentence for homicide by vehicle while driving while under the influence because the former offense was a lesser-included offense of the later. The trial court did not error when it did not suppress results of a blood alcohol test. Trial counsel was not ineffective.

Commonwealth v. Hatfield


No. 03012 Philadelphia, 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


397 Pa. Super. 90; 579 A.2d 945; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2641


June 11, 1990, Argued  
September 4, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Entered September 21, 1989, in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, at No. 6012-88.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which entered a judgment of sentence following his conviction on two counts of both robbery and aggravated assault, and one count each of first degree murder, burglary, theft, receiving stolen property, and possessing an instrument of crime. Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: An officer stopped defendant after he had broken into a residence. Defendant attacked the officer after he put his hand on his gun and, during the struggle, defendant grabbed the gun and shot the officer, fatally wounding him. Defendant was convicted of two counts of both robbery and aggravated assault and one count each of first-degree murder, burglary, theft, receiving stolen property, and possessing an instrument of crime. The trial court sentenced him to life imprisonment. On appeal defendant challenged the constitutionality of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 314. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that § 314's distinction between defendants who presented an insanity defense and those who did not was not violative of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court determined that because § 314 indicated that mental illness was not at issue absent an insanity defense, defendant's evidence of mental illness was irrelevant and inadmissible. The court concluded that § 314, which by implication permitted only those defendants who raised the defense of insanity to introduce evidence of mental illness, bore a rational relationship to the legislative purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence, holding that the statute permitting only those defendants who raised the defense of insanity to introduce evidence of mental illness was constitutional. The court found that defendant's mental illness was not at issue absent an insanity defense, and therefore evidence of his mental illness was irrelevant and inadmissible.

Rivera v. Philadelphia Theological Seminary of St. Charles Borromeo, Inc.


Nos. 1776 Philadelphia 1989, 2023 Philadelphia 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


398 Pa. Super. 264; 580 A.2d 1341; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2553


January 23, 1990, Argued  
August 30, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied October 15, 1990. Appeal Denied July 15, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant seminary challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which entered a jury verdict determining its percentage of liability as compared to appellee church for the wrongful death of appellee administratrix' son. The trial court awarded 10 percent interest as damages for delay under Pa. R. Civ. P. 238.


OVERVIEW: Appellee administratrix was the mother of a 12-year-old boy who drowned in a swimming pool belonging to appellant seminary and supervised by appellee church. After appellee administratrix brought a successful wrongful death action, appellant won a new trial as against appellee church only to determine its percentage of liability. The jury then set appellant's liability at 31.6 percent, and damages for delay were added at a rate of 10 percent interest. Appellant sought review but the court affirmed the judgment, finding that the jury instruction on the duty of care for the owner of a swimming pool was correct where it relied on the reasonable precautions that the owner should take as to foreseeable users of the pool. The court further found that the participation by appellee administratrix in the second trial was permissible and that the improper remark made by her counsel regarding the outcome of the first trial was properly cured by the trial court. The court, however, modified the judgment to remove the damages for delay under Pa. R. Civ. P. 238 and to reinstate the rate of 6 percent interest that had already been set on the total judgment under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8101.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment entered against appellant seminary that determined its percentage of liability compared to appellee church on the suit by appellee administratrix for the wrongful death of her son. The court, however, modified the judgment by reinstating the statutory 6 percent rate of interest that was already accumulating on the judgment.

Commonwealth v. Stranges


No. 3419 Philadelphia, 1988


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


397 Pa. Super. 59; 579 A.2d 930; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2412


May 31, 1990, Argued  
August 24, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended. Appeal Denied January 4, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order entered November 15, 1988, Court of Common Pleas, Bucks County, Criminal Division, at No. 2692/88.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed. Case remanded. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which reversed its decision denying appellee drunk driver's admission into the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition program.


OVERVIEW: Appellee drunk driver was charged with driving under the influence of alcohol, 75 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 3731(a)(1), (4). Appellee filed an application for admission into the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition (ARD), which application the district attorney for appellant Commonwealth denied. The trial court found that the district attorney abused his discretion, and ordered that appellee be admitted to the ARD program. The appellate court reversed, holding that the district attorney's decision not to admit appellee to the ARD was not an abuse of discretion, as it was based upon a general policy bearing some relationship to the protection of the public. Specifically, the district attorney had a policy of withholding ARD from any person charged with drunk driving who was involved in an accident with another occupied vehicle, regardless of fault. Facts that appellee had no prior contacts with the criminal justice system, was not at fault for the accident, and was recommended for the ARD program by the investigating officer, were irrelevant.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's order, which ordered appellant Commonwealth to admit appellee into the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition Program, because appellant's denial of appellee for admission was not an abuse of discretion, as it was based upon a general policy bearing some relationship to the protection of the public.

Commonwealth v. Myrtetus


No. 00390 Philadelphia, 1990


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


397 Pa. Super. 299; 580 A.2d 42; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2408


July 23, 1990, Submitted  
August 22, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, Criminal Division, No. 4440-89.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania) for driving under the influence of alcohol under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(4) after being stopped at a roadblock set up under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308(b).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(4) after being stopped at a roadblock set up under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6308(b). Appellant challenged his conviction, but the court affirmed the judgment and sentence, finding that the roadblock was a constitutional search because it was conducted according to guidelines that kept the stops systematic and kept the questions asked to a minimum. The court further found that appellant could not introduce into evidence his pre-arrest breath test results under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(k) because those results came from equipment not calibrated or tested for accuracy, and were only intended to be used to assist the police in determining probable cause for arrest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol, finding that the roadblock was constitutional and properly conducted because the stops were systematic, uniform, and questions were kept to a minimum. The court also found that appellant could not introduce his pre-arrest breath test results because they were the result of using uncalibrated equipment.

Commonwealth v. Brown


No. 405 Philadelphia, 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


396 Pa. Super. 171; 578 A.2d 461; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1757


June 19, 1990, Submitted  
July 24, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a judgment of sentence from a trial court (Pennsylvania) entered after appellant was convicted of first-degree murder, conspiracy, aggravated assault, and robbery.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested on a federal fugitive warrant. Appellant pleaded guilty to murder, but later withdrew the plea. Appellant also waived his right to a speedy trial. Appellant was convicted on all counts and he was sentenced to life imprisonment. Appellant claimed that his right to a speedy trial was violated because the trial court did not view the withdrawal of his guilty plea as triggering the period in which the prosecution had to bring him to trial. On appeal, the court found that appellant's rights were not violated because he was not prejudiced. The court held that a detailed description of appellant's past bizarre behavior was not relevant to his alleged diminished capacity at the time of the criminal act. Additionally, the court held that appellant's incriminating statements made after a knowing and voluntary waiver of Miranda rights were admissible because there was support in the record for the trial court's finding that appellant made he statements. The court also held that appellant waived his right to challenged the array of potential jurors because his objection was not in accordance with the procedure set out in Pa. R. Crim. P. 1104. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment of sentence entered after appellant was convicted of multiple offenses was affirmed. Appellant's right to a speedy trial was not violated, the trial court did not err by preventing testimony on diminished capacity, appellant's statements were properly admitted, and he was denied the right to a fair trial.

Commonwealth v. Chilcote


No. 00133 Harrisburg 1987


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


396 Pa. Super. 106; 578 A.2d 429; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1407


January 16, 1990, Submitted  
July 20, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied April 29, 1991. Petition for Reconsideration Granted June 17, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction after a nonjury trial and sentence from a court in Pennsylvania for possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver, in violation of 35 P. S. § 780-113(a)(30), after his original sentence of eight to twenty years imprisonment was reduced to an aggregate term of seven years in response to his motion to modify sentence, and his second motion to modify was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for four counts of possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver as a result of the sale by defendant to an undercover officer. The court affirmed the conviction on appeal. The court held that through the filing of his pre-trial motion and subsequent appeals, defendant implicitly requested that trial be delayed until the issue was resolved since a favorable decision could eliminate the necessity of a criminal trial. For purposes of calculating the 180-day period, defendant was unavailable for trial for the period of time in which the issue of whether a physician would be appointed under 35 P.S. § 780-118(a) was pending before the trial court as a pretrial motion. The period of time that defendant's prior appeal was pending before the court and the supreme court was also excludable. There was no violation of defendant's due process rights under the federal and state constitutions as disposition under 35 P.S. § 780-118 was not a fundamental constitutional right.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver was affirmed on the grounds that defendant's right to a speedy trial was not violated, and there was no error in the sentence imposed by the trial court.

Commonwealth v. One 1986 Ford Mustang "EXP"


No. 02560 Philadelphia 1989


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


397 Pa. Super. 116; 579 A.2d 958; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2645


April 2, 1990, Submitted  
July 10, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Berks County, No. MD 23/89.

This Opinion Substituted by Court for Withdrawn Opinion of July 10, 1990, Reported at 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2121.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania) which seized his vehicle pursuant to the Controlled Substances Forfeiture Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6801 and 6802. Appellant argued that the seizure of his vehicle and subsequent forfeiture to the commonwealth violated various federal and state constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant individual and a passenger in his automobile were arrested and charged with possession of cocaine and various drug paraphernalia. The Commonwealth seized appellant's vehicle pursuant to the Controlled Substances Forfeitures Act (Act), 42 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 6801 and 6802. Appellant argued that the seizure of his vehicle and subsequent forfeiture violated various federal and state constitutional rights. The lower court decision was affirmed. The court held that the controlled substances were in "plain view" of the arresting officers and thus legally confiscated. Appellant's contention that the Act violated due process was without merit because appellant never raised the issue in the lower court. Appellant was not unconstitutionally denied a jury trial because the right to a jury trial in a forfeiture proceeding did not exist at the adoption of the state constitution. The forfeiture of appellant's vehicle pursuant to the Act was not an excessive penalty. The Act provided for the forfeiture of a vehicle that was used to facilitate the transport, sale, receipt, possession or concealment of drug paraphernalia and controlled substances, without regard to amount.


OUTCOME: The lower court decision that ordered the seizure of appellant's vehicle pursuant to the controlled substances forfeitures act was affirmed. The court held, among other things, that the controlled substances were in "plain view" of the arresting officers and thus legally confiscated and forfeiture was not prohibited merely because many of the drugs possessed in the vehicle had been consumed or sold prior to the seizure.

Commonwealth v. Pontious


Nos. 01182 Pittsburgh, 1988, 01232 Pittsburgh, 1988


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


396 Pa. Super. 15; 578 A.2d 1; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1382


March 30, 1990, Argued  
July 5, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the orders from trial courts (Pennsylvania), which suppressed evidence in driving under the influence proceedings that were obtained by a police officer who had been hired in violation of the civil service provision of the Borough Code, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 45101-48501 (1989).


OVERVIEW: Two defendants were arrested by the same officer for driving under the influence. The trial court granted defendants' motions to suppress the evidence obtained by the officer, holding that the arrests were unlawful because the arresting officer had been hired in violation of the civil service provision of the Borough Code, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 46171 (1989). On consolidated appeal, the court of appeals reversed. The court held that defendants did not have standing to challenge the alleged violation of § 46171. The court found that even if the officer had been illegally hired, that did not convert his legal arrests of defendants into illegal arrests.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the suppression orders of the trial court. The court held that defendants lacked standing to challenge the arresting officer's employment in violation of the civil service law and the trial court erred by suppressing the evidence as fruit of illegal arrests.

 

400 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Novelli v. Johns-Manville Corp.


Nos. 02712 PHL 89, 02713 PHL 89, 02807 PHL 89


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


395 Pa. Super. 144; 576 A.2d 1085; 1990 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1000


March 22, 1990, Argued  
June 19, 1990, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife challenged an order and judgment from a trial court (Pennsylvania) that awarded her damages in her and her deceased husband's suit to recover for his asbestos-related injuries and her loss of consortium. Plaintiff claimed that her damages for loss of consortium were measured improperly. By cross-appeal, defendant manufacturer claimed that the trial court erred in awarding Pa. R. Civ. P. 238 delay damages on the consortium claim.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff wife appealed an order and judgment that, in a successful suit brought by her and her husband to recover damages for husband's asbestos-related injuries and her loss of consortium, limited her recovery for loss of consortium to the date of husband's death. Defendant manufacturer, by cross-appeal, argued that delay damages were improperly predicated upon the loss of consortium claim because loss of consortium was not a claim "for bodily injury, death, or property damage within the meaning of Pa. R. Civ. P. 238 (rule 238). The court affirmed the judgment and order. Regarding plaintiff's claim, the court ruled that plaintiff offered no compelling reason for departing from the traditional limitation that limited recovery for harm to her interests and expense incurred between the injury and death of her husband. In response to defendant's claim, the court ruled that because the loss of consortium claim was inextricably intertwined with husband's underlying personal injury action, and because the underlying purpose of rule 238 was to prevent unreasonable refusals to pay what was owed for tortious injury, in light of defendant's refusal to settle, rule 238 damages were proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment and order that awarded plaintiff wife damages for loss of consortium and awarding delay damages for the portion of the verdict that represented the recovery for loss of consortium. Plaintiff's damages for loss of consortium were properly not extended beyond the date of her husband's death. Delay damages were properly awarded because loss of consortium was a claim for bodily injury, death, or property damage.
